Qidam as a Positive Attribute: Al-Razi's Defense
Al-Razi refutes the definition of qidam as purely negative and defends it as a positive attribute, addressing regress and using analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi defends the view that qidam (eternity) is a positive attribute (amr wujudi) and how he counters objections about infinite regress using a key distinction.

Core Idea
Qidam is not mere negation but a positive attribute of not being preceded by non-existence.

In this passage, al-Razi tackles a major objection against the reality of divine attributes: that qidam is merely a negative concept. He transforms the definition to show it entails a positive state, then defends this view against a regress argument, and finally uses an analogy from created things to reinforce his point. This lesson will walk you through his multi-layered reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how al-Razi first rejects the opponent's definition, then anticipates a counter-argument, responds, and finally concedes a point to strengthen his case.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا نسلم' — it signals a rejection of a premise.
2
Identify the opponent's initial claim
The opponent says qidam is an expression for the negation of prior non-existence. Al-Razi redefines it as the negation of being preceded by non-existence. Why is this shift important?
The key is that 'being preceded' is a positive state, not a pure absence.
3
Trace the infinite regress objection and al-Razi's reply
The opponent argues: if 'being preceded' is a positive attribute, then it either is eternal (making the essence originated) or originated (leading to regress). Al-Razi counters by pointing out that such precedence is a real distinguishable attribute of existence, so the regress argument fails.
Al-Razi uses a 'tanaqud' (contradiction) approach: the opponent's own logic would lead to absurdity.
4
Understand the analogy with temporal events
Al-Razi concedes that qidam is positive (for argument) and then gives an example: temporal events differ in essence but share the attribute of being originated. Similarly, the divine essence and attribute may share qidam while being distinct.
This analogy undermines the opponent's claim that attributes cannot be real if they share a common attribute.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Review how al-Razi uses logical steps to defend the reality of attributes. The key is that qidam involves a positive relational attribute (precedence) and that sharing a common attribute does not erase distinction.
This passage is a good example of kalam dialectic: definition, objection, counter-objection, and analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم الرابعة : أنا لا نسلم أن القدم مفهوم ثبوتي. قوله : القدم عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق قلنا لا نسلم بل هو عبارة عن نفي كون الشيء مسبوقاً بذلك العدم، وكونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمر وجودي. لا يقال لو كان كونه مسبوقا بالعدم أمرًا وجوديًا لكان ذلك الزائد إما أن يكون قديما فيلزم أن تكون الذات محدثة والصفة قديمة وهو محال، أو يكون محدثاً فيكون مسبوقاً بالعدم فتكون المسبوقية زائدة عليه، ولزم التسلسل، لأنا نقول: هذا معارض من وجه آخر وهو أن مسبوقية وجوده بالعدم صفة من صفات وجوده، ونحن ندرك بالضرورة تفرقة بين هذه الصفة وبين نفس العدم المحض، فوجب أن تكون هذه المسبوقية صفة ثبوتية. سلمنا أن القدم الأمر ثبوتي فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : الذات والصفة حقيقتان مختلفتان لذاتيهما إلا أنهما مشتركتان في هذا القدم؟ والأشياء المختلفة لا يبعد في العقل اشتراكها في لازم واحد والذي يحقق ما ذكرناه أن الحوادث مختلفة في الماهيات ومشتركة في كونها حادثة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في القدم؟
And the answer to their fourth doubt: We do not accept that qidam is a positive concept. He said: 'Qidam is an expression for the negation of prior non-existence.' We say: We do not accept; rather it is an expression for the negation of the thing being preceded by that non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is an existent matter (amr wujudi). It cannot be said: If its being preceded by non-existence were an existent matter, then that extra (attribute) would either be eternal, necessitating that the essence is originated and the attribute eternal, which is impossible; or it would be originated, thus being preceded by non-existence, so the precedence would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. For we say: This is countered from another angle: that the precedence of its existence by non-existence is an attribute of its existence, and we perceive by necessity a distinction between this attribute and pure non-existence itself. So this precedence must be a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya). We concede that qidam is a positive thing. Then why can it not be said that the essence and the attribute are two realities distinct in themselves except that they share in this qidam? Different things are not rationally prevented from sharing a single necessary concomitant (lazim). What confirms our point is that temporal events differ in essences and share in being originated (haditha). So why can it not be the case with qidam?
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Al-Razi responds to the fourth objection against the reality of divine attributes. The opponent argues that qidam (eternity) is a negative concept, i.e., merely the absence of prior non-existence. Al-Razi first rejects that premise: qidam is not a negation of non-existence; rather it is the negation of being preceded by non-existence, which is a positive state ('being preceded' is an existent attribute). He then anticipates an infinite regress objection: if this 'precedence' is a positive attribute, then either it is eternal (making the essence originated) or originated (leading to regress). Al-Razi counters by pointing out that precedence is a real attribute of existence, distinguishable from pure non-existence, so it must be positive. The regress argument is thus neutralized. Finally, he concedes for argument's sake that qidam is positive and uses it to show that distinct essences can share a common attribute, like temporal events sharing originatedness. This undermines the opponent's claim that attributes cannot be real.

Key Terms
القدم (qidam)
ثبوتي (thubuti)
مسبوقية (precedence)
صفة ثبوتية (positive attribute)
لازم (lazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam (eternity) is a purely negative concept, equivalent to absence of beginning or non-existence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that qidam is a positive attribute because it entails the negation of being preceded by non-existence, and 'being preceded' is an existent matter (amr wujudi).

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's response, what does qidam (eternity) truly express?
A) The negation of non-existence altogether
B) The negation of being preceded by non-existence, which is a positive state
C) The absence of beginning
D) A pure negation

Key Takeaway
Qidam is a positive attribute because it involves the real state of not being preceded by non-existence, and distinct realities can share such an attribute, as seen in temporal events sharing originatedness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is eternal,' do you actually feel that as a positive reality of His being, or is it just the absence of a beginning in your mind? How does al-Razi's insistence that qidam is thubūtī challenge you to deepen your understanding of God's attributes beyond mere negations?
?
Al-Razi uses an analogy: temporal events share 'originatedness' despite different essences. If you truly internalize that even created things share attributes, how should that affect your humility before God when you affirm that His attributes are real and distinct from His essence? Are you willing to accept that your mind cannot fully grasp this, yet you must affirm it as true?
?
This argument shows that we perceive a distinction between precedence and non-existence by necessity. Is there an area of your faith where you have reduced a positive truth about Allah to a mere negation to avoid intellectual discomfort? What would it mean for your worship to treat each of His attributes as a positive, real quality that demands a response from you?

The Attribute is Neither He Nor Other: A Verbal Dispute
Al-Razi clarifies that the dispute about attributes being identical or distinct is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi resolves the dispute over the attribute-essence relationship by clarifying the ambiguous term 'two things'.

Core Idea
What seems like a contradiction is actually a confusion about language.

Al-Razi now tackles the fifth objection, where opponents find the Ash'ari formula 'neither He nor other' contradictory. He argues that the dispute is merely verbal, depending on what 'two things' means. Let's see his reasoning and how disambiguation dissolves the apparent problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice al-Razi's structure: he states the Ash'ari position, then offers two conditional interpretations.
The phrase 'لا هو ولا غيره' is the core of the dispute.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'ghayrayn'
Al-Razi considers two definitions of 'two things': (1) independent entities, (2) any two distinguishable aspects. Identify how the answer changes under each.
This is the key move in the argument.
3
See how the objection dissolves
Under the first meaning, attributes are not 'two' with the essence; under the second, the objection loses its force. No real contradiction remains.
Al-Razi concludes the dispute is merely verbal.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how clarifying terms can resolve theological disagreements. This is a common kalam technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم الخامسة : أن أصحابنا قالوا : الصفة مع الذات لا هو ولا غيره واستبعد المخالفون ذلك، وهذا البحث لفظي، فإنا نقول : إن عنيتم بالغيرين أشياء مستقلة بالذات والحقيقة فلا نسلم أن الذات والصفة غيران على هذا التفسير، وإن عنيتم بالغيرين كل شيئين سواء كانا مستقلين أو كان أحدهما صفة للآخر، والآخر موصوفًا به فلم قلتم : إن لإثبات شيئين متغايرين قديمين بهذا التفسير باطل، فإن النزاع ما وقع إلا فيه ؟
And the answer to their fifth doubt: Our companions said: The attribute with the essence is neither It nor other than It. The opponents found this objectionable. This is a verbal dispute. So we say: If by 'two things' (ghayrayn) you mean entities independent in essence and reality, then we do not accept that the essence and the attribute are two under this interpretation. If by 'two things' you mean any two things, whether they are independent or one is an attribute of the other and the other is described by it, then why do you say that establishing two mutually distinct eternal things under this interpretation is false? For the dispute only occurred about this.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit on the relationship between essence and attributes. The Ash'arite position is that the attribute is neither identical to the essence nor completely distinct (la huwa wa la ghayruhu). Opponents find this contradictory. Al-Razi argues that this is a verbal dispute (bahth lafzi). He clarifies: if 'two things' means independently existing entities, then attributes are not 'two' with the essence. If 'two things' means any two distinguishable aspects, even if one is a dependent attribute, then the question becomes whether affirming two eternal entities in this sense is false. Al-Razi implies that the real disagreement is about the first interpretation, not the second, so the objection is misguided.

Key Terms
لا هو ولا غيره
غيران
صفة
ذات
بحث لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'la huwa wa la ghayruhu' is a logical contradiction.
Correction: It is not contradictory; it means the attribute is not a separate independent entity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is the objection about the attribute being 'neither He nor other' a verbal dispute?
A) Because opponents misunderstand the Arabic language
B) Because the meaning of 'two things' (ghayrayn) is ambiguous
C) Because attributes are actually identical to the essence
D) Because there is no real difference between the two positions

Key Takeaway
Disputes in kalam often dissolve when ambiguous terms are clarified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'Allah's attributes are neither Him nor other than Him,' does your mind resist because it feels like a contradiction? What does that resistance reveal about your expectation that Allah must fit into your logical categories? Are you willing to accept that He transcends them, even if it leaves you uncomfortable?
?
Think of a recent argument you had about Islam—was it truly about substance or about definitions? How many of your religious disagreements would dissolve if both sides clarified what they meant by key terms? What would it cost you to pursue clarity rather than winning?
?
If the Ash'ari position is that attributes are not separate from Allah's essence, how does that affect the way you relate to His names and attributes in your prayer and daily life? Do you treat them as realities of His being, or as abstract concepts? What would change if you genuinely believed that His mercy, power, and justice are not other than Him?

Distinguishing Islamic Attributes from Christian Hypostases
Al-Razi answers the sixth objection by distinguishing Islamic attributes from Christian hypostases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi distinguishes Islamic attributes from Christian hypostases by demonstrating that Christians treat attributes as independent essences.

Core Idea
Islamic attributes are not independent substances; Christian hypostases are.

Al-Razi addresses a key objection: if Muslims affirm eternal attributes, why aren't they like Christians? He responds by showing that Christians treat attributes as independent essences (dhawat), which Muslims do not. This passage contrasts the two views and defends Islamic monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: Al-Razi begins with God's judgment on Christians, then provides evidence, then concludes.
Pay attention to the key terms: ذوات (essences) and أقنوم الكلمة (hypostasis of the Word).
2
Identify the argument
Al-Razi argues that Christians are disbelievers because they treat attributes as independent substances. The evidence is their doctrine of the transfer of the hypostasis of the Word from God to Jesus.
The transfer implies independence—a true attribute cannot be transferred independently.
3
Contrast with Islamic view
Reflect on the key distinction: Muslims affirm attributes that are not independent—they are inseparable from God's essence. Thus, affirming multiplicity in attributes does not imply polytheism.
This answers the objection that Islamic attributes are like Christian hypostases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن شبهتهم السادسة أن الله تعالى كفر النصارى لأنهم أثبتوا صفات الحقيقة ذوات ألا ترى أنهم جوزوا أن انتقال أقنوم الكلمة من ذات الله إلى بدن عيسى عليه السلام والشيء الذي يكون مستقلا بالانتقال من ذات إلى ذات أخرى يكون مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته . فهم وإن سموها صفات إلا أنهم قائلون في الحقيقة بكونها ذوات، ومن أثبت كثرة في الذوات المستقلة بأنفسها فلا شك في كفره، فلم قلتم : إن من أثبت الكثرة في الصفات لزمه الكفر؟
And the answer to their sixth doubt: God Most High declared the Christians disbelievers because they affirmed attributes as real essences (dhawat). Do you not see that they allowed the transfer of the hypostasis of the Word from the essence of God to the body of Jesus (peace be upon him)? And a thing that can independently transfer from one essence to another is independent in itself, subsisting by itself. So, even if they call them attributes, they are in reality affirming them as essences. And whoever affirms multiplicity in essences independent in themselves, there is no doubt in his disbelief. So why do you say that whoever affirms multiplicity in attributes necessarily becomes a disbeliever?
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit drawing a contrast between Islamic and Christian views. The opponent argues that affirming eternal attributes leads to polytheism like Christianity. Al-Razi responds: God declared Christians disbelievers because they believed attributes are independent substances (dhawat). Evidence is their doctrine of the hypostasis (uqnūm) of the Word transferring from God to Jesus' body, implying independence. Christians may call them 'attributes', but they treat them as independent entities. Affirming multiplicity in independent essences is disbelief. However, Muslims affirm attributes that are not independent — they are not like Christian hypostases. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
ذوات (dhawat/essences)
أقنوم الكلمة (hypostasis of the Word)
صفات (attributes)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islamic eternal attributes are like Christian hypostases because both involve multiple eternal entities.
Correction: Christians treat attributes as independent essences (dhawat) that can transfer, whereas Islamic attributes are not independent—they are inseparable from God's essence and do not act as separate substances.

Checkpoint
Why did God declare Christians disbelievers according to al-Razi?
A) Because they believed in three attributes
B) Because they considered attributes to be independent essences (dhawat)
C) Because they denied the oneness of God
D) Because they called attributes by different names

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that Christians are declared disbelievers because they treat attributes as independent essences, unlike the Islamic view where attributes are not independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that simply calling something an 'attribute' makes it safe from shirk? How does Al-Razi's warning about the Christians—who also called them 'attributes'—expose the danger of trusting labels over reality? What labels in your own heart might you be hiding behind?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' or 'parts' of God? What steps will you take today to train your heart to see them as nothing other than His perfect reality, and to worship Him through them without division?
?
The Christians allowed the 'transfer' of the Word to Jesus. Are there any beliefs in your own life that 'transfer' what belongs exclusively to Allah to someone or something else—even subtly—such as relying on creation for what only the Creator can give? Will you repent from that right now and reaffirm that every attribute and every action belongs to Him alone?

Four Responses to the Anthropomorphic Objection About God's Knowledge
Al-Razi enumerates four responses to the objection that God's knowledge is like human knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical rebuttals that Imam al-Razi uses to defend God's knowledge against the anthropomorphic objection.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not like human knowledge, even if both relate to the same object.

Imam al-Razi presents four incisive responses to the argument that God's knowledge must be similar to human knowledge because both know the same thing. These rebuttals employ concepts like necessary concomitants, the distinction between knowledge and the attribute of being knowing, and analogical reasoning about existence. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection being answered
The objector claims that if God and humans both know the same fact, then their knowledge must be similar, leading to problematic implications. Al-Razi's responses aim to break this inference.
2
Analyze the first response: sharing a necessary concomitant does not imply similarity
Two different things can share a common property without being alike in essence. Read the first argument carefully and note the role of the 'necessary concomitant' (lazim).
3
Examine the second response: redefining the relation
Al-Razi suggests that it is not knowledge but the attribute 'being knowing' (al-ālimiyya) that relates to the object, undermining the objector's premise.
4
Study the third and fourth responses: tu quoque and the analogy of existence
The third response turns the argument against the opponent; the fourth allows that even if there is similarity in some respect, it does not entail equality in essential attributes like eternity.
The example of existence having one concept but differing in duration is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشبهة الأولى : فالجواب عنها بوجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد و على طريقة واحدة فإنهما متساويان في هذا التعلق، وهذا التعلق من لوازمهما، وقد ثبت أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في العقل في لازم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من استواء العلمين في هذا القدر تماثلهما ؟ فإن قالوا : فإذا لم يلزم من هذا القدر تماثل العلمين فبأي طريق تعرفون تماثل العلمين في الشاهد؟ فنقول : قد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يقتضي الجزم بالتماثل، وإن لم يحصل في الشاهد إلا هذا الطريق وجب أن لا يقطع في الشاهد أيضا بالتماثل بل يتوقف فيه، وإن حصل فيه طريق آخر سوى هذا الطريق قطعنا به وهذا جواب قاطع. الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم في الشاهد غير متعلق بالمعلوم، إنما المتعلق بالمعلوم هو العالمية وإذا لم يكن العلم متعلقا بالمعلوم بطل قولهم العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة يوجب القول بكونهما متماثلين. الثالث : أن ما ألزموه علينا في العلمين يلزمهم في العالميتين، فإن عالمية الله تعالى وعالميتنا قد تعلقتا بالمعلوم الواحد من الوجه الواحد على الطريقة الواحدة فيلزمهم أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى مثلا لعالمية واحد منا وتلزمهم المحالات المذكورة. والرابع : هب أنه يلزم أن يكون علم الله تعالى مثلا لعلم الواحد منا ، لكن لم يلزم من هذا القدر التساوي في القدم والحدوث؟ أليس أن الوجود في الشاهد والغائب له حقيقة واحد ومعقول واحد مع أن الوجود في الشاهد متجدد، وفي الغائب دائم ؟ فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في المعلوم؟
As for the first doubt, the answer to it has several aspects: First: Why may it not be said that two knowledges pertaining to one object of knowledge in one respect and one manner are equal in this relation, and this relation is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of them? It has been established that things different in essence are not prevented in reason from sharing a single necessary concomitant. If so, it does not follow from the equality of the two knowledges in this respect that they are similar. If they say: If similarity does not follow from this, then by what means do you know the similarity of two knowledges in the witnessed world? We say: We have shown that this does not necessitate certainty of similarity. Even if in the witnessed world only this way exists, one must not be certain of similarity in the witnessed world either; rather one should withhold judgment. If there is another way besides this, we judge by it, and this is a conclusive answer. Second: Why may it not be said that knowledge in the witnessed world is not related to the object of knowledge; rather what is related to the object of knowledge is 'being knowing' (al-ālimiyya). And if knowledge is not related to the object, their claim that two knowledges related to one object in one respect and manner necessitate their similarity falls away. Third: What they impose on us concerning two knowledges applies to them concerning two 'being-knowing' attributes. For God's 'being knowing' and our 'being knowing' relate to one object of knowledge in one respect and one manner, so it would be necessary for them that God's 'being knowing' is like that of one of us, and the mentioned impossibilities would follow. Fourth: Grant that it necessarily follows that God's knowledge is like the knowledge of one of us; but does it follow from this equality in pre-eternity and temporal origination? Is it not that existence in the witnessed and absent has a single reality and a single concept, while existence in the witnessed is temporal and in the absent perpetual? Why may it not be the case with knowledge?
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part response to the first objection against God's knowledge. The objection presumably claims that if God and humans both know the same thing, their knowledge must be similar, leading to anthropomorphism. Al-Razi gives four rebuttals. First: two knowledges can share a relation without being similar, because different things can share a common necessary concomitant. Second: he redefines the relation: perhaps knowledge itself is not related to the object; rather the attribute of 'being knowing' (ālimiyya) is related, so the objection's premise fails. Third: he turns the tables: the same argument would force the opponent to say God's attribute of being knowing is like human knowing, which is absurd. Fourth: even if knowledge is similar in some respect, it does not imply equality in essential attributes like eternity vs. temporality; just as existence has one concept but differs in duration, so can knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
العالمية
تماثل
لازم
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two knowledges relate to the same object in the same respect, they must be similar.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that different essences can share a necessary concomitant without being similar, and that the relation may not even be between knowledge itself and the object.

Checkpoint
In the first response to the objection about God's knowledge, what does al-Razi argue about two knowledges related to the same object?
A) They must be exactly similar
B) They are unrelated because God's knowledge is unknowable
C) They can share a relation without being similar because different essences can share a necessary concomitant
D) They are identical to the object itself

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's four responses demonstrate that the objector's inference from shared relation to similarity is invalid, preserving the transcendence of divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God knows everything', do you unconsciously imagine God's knowledge as a bigger version of your own? How does al-Razi's first response challenge that mental image? Can you commit to remembering that God's knowledge is not like yours, even in its relation to what you know?
?
Which of the four responses do you find most personally convincing? Why? If you were to explain to someone that God's knowledge is not like human knowledge, which response would you use? What does that say about your own assumptions?
?
Al-Razi uses the analogy of existence: existence is one concept but differs in duration. How does this affect your understanding of attributes that God and humans share? Are you willing to accept that even when you use the same word ('knowledge'), the reality can be radically different?

4 min
Turning the Trilemma Back on the Opponent
Al-Razi turns the trilemma back on the opponent's al-ālimiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi neutralizes the trilemma about God's knowledge by applying the same logical structure to the opponent's concept of al-ālimiyya.

Core Idea
The trilemma about knowledge is a mirror for the opponent's own attribute.

Al-Razi has just answered another objection; now he turns to the second doubt. The opponent presents a trilemma: God's knowledge of infinite objects must be one, finite, or infinite—each problematic. Instead of choosing, al-Razi shows that the same difficulty applies to the opponent's concept of 'being knowing' (ālimiyya). This clever refutation shifts the burden back.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's trilemma, then al-Razi's response.
The response is very concise—only two sentences.
2
Understand the opponent's trilemma
The opponent claims that God's knowledge of infinite things must be either one, finite, or infinite, and each has problems. Identify these three options.
3
See how al-Razi mirrors it
Al-Razi says: 'This doubt in its entirety applies to you concerning al-ālimiyya.' That means the same trilemma applies to the attribute of being knowing. Ask yourself: why is this a valid rebuttal?
Hint: The opponent also affirms that God is 'ālim (knowing) in a way that relates to infinite objects.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether this refutation actually solves the problem or just shifts it. What does it show about the nature of kalam debates?
This is a classic dialectical move: turning the tables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : - وهي قولهم: إما أن يعلم المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحد أو بعلوم متناهية أو بعلوم غير متناهية - فنقول : هذه الشبهة بتمامها واردة عليكم في العالمية، وكل ما تقولونه في العالمية نقوله في العلم.
And as for the second doubt - which is their saying: 'Either He knows the infinite objects of knowledge by one knowledge, or by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges' - we say: This doubt in its entirety applies to you concerning al-ālimiyya (the attribute of being knowing). And whatever you say about al-ālimiyya, we say about knowledge.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a concise rebuttal (tuq) to the second objection. The opponent presents a trilemma: God's knowledge of infinite things must be either a single knowledge, finite knowledges, or infinite knowledges, each problematic. Al-Razi replies that exactly the same trilemma applies to the opponent's concept of 'ālimiyya (the attribute of being knowing). Whatever difficulty they raise about knowledge, the same can be raised about being knowing. Thus the objection is neutralized; it does not specifically refute the Ash'arite position.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
العالمية (being knowing)
معلومات (objects of knowledge)
متناهية (finite)
غير متناهية (infinite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The trilemma about God's knowledge is unique to the Ash'arite theory of a single attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the same trilemma applies equally to the opponent's concept of al-ālimiyya, so it is not a unique problem.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi respond to the trilemma about God knowing infinite objects?
A) He chooses one of the three options
B) He denies that God knows infinite things
C) He argues the same trilemma applies to the attribute of 'being knowing' (ālimiyya)
D) He says it is a false dilemma

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi neutralizes the objection by showing that the same trilemma applies to the opponent's own concept of al-ālimiyya, so it is not a special problem for his position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone criticizes your belief, do you immediately defend yourself, or do you first consider whether their criticism applies to their own position? Recall a specific debate or discussion where you could have used this redirect technique. How would that have changed the outcome?
?
Think of a personal struggle you think is unique to you. Is it possible that others face the same struggle, and that your problem is not as special as you think? This week, when you feel burdened, ask yourself: 'Is this truly unique to me, or is it a universal human experience?' How might that realization bring you peace?

Divine Attributes

8 min
First Answer: Equality in Relation Does Not Imply Similarity
First answer to the first doubt about God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two knowledges relating to the same known in exactly the same manner do not necessarily have to be similar, and how Razi defends this by appealing to the distinction between a necessary concomitant and essential similarity.

Core Idea
Different essences can share a concomitant without being similar.

The first doubt about God's knowledge argues that if God's knowledge and human knowledge both relate to the same known in the same way, they must be similar. Imam Razi's first answer challenges this inference. He points out that a shared relation is a necessary concomitant, not a proof of essential similarity. Different things can share a property without being alike in essence. He then responds to a potential objection by showing that in the witnessed realm we cannot be certain of similarity solely from this shared relation, and must either suspend judgment or find another criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Read the Arabic passage for the first answer and its translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument: he first states the opponent's position, then provides his counterpoint, and finally answers an objection.
The passage includes a hypothetical objection from the opponent.
2
Identify the key logical move
Identify the key premise: 'things different in quiddity are not impossible to share in the intellect in one concomitant'. This is crucial. Understand that a concomitant (lāzim) is a property that necessarily follows from something but is not identical to its essence.
3
Examine Razi's reply to the objection
The opponent asks: if this relation doesn't prove similarity, then how do you know similarity in the witnessed realm? Razi says: either we suspend judgment or we have another criterion. This shows that the burden of proof is on the opponent.
This is a decisive answer (jawāb qāṭiʿ).
4
Reflect on the implication for divine knowledge
Consider how this argument protects the uniqueness of God's knowledge. If similarity is not proven, then God's knowledge may be of a different quiddity, preserving divine transcendence.
This answer does not prove God's knowledge is different, but blocks the argument that it must be similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الأولى : فالجواب عنها بوجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد و على طريقة واحدة فإنهما متساويان في هذا التعلق، وهذا التعلق من لوازمهما، وقد ثبت أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في العقل في لازم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من استواء العلمين في هذا القدر تماثلهما ؟ فإن قالوا : فإذا لم يلزم من هذا القدر تماثل العلمين فبأي طريق تعرفون تماثل العلمين في الشاهد؟ فنقول : قد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يقتضي الجزم بالتماثل، وإن لم يحصل في الشاهد إلا هذا الطريق وجب أن لا يقطع في الشاهد أيضا بالتماثل بل يتوقف فيه، وإن حصل فيه طريق آخر سوى هذا الطريق قطعنا به وهذا جواب قاطع. الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم في الشاهد غير متعلق بالمعلوم، إنما المتعلق بالمعلوم هو العالمية وإذا لم يكن العلم متعلقا بالمعلوم بطل قولهم العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة يوجب القول بكونهما متماثلين. الثالث : أن ما ألزموه علينا في العلمين يلزمهم في العالميتين، فإن عالمية الله تعالى وعالميتنا قد تعلقتا بالمعلوم الواحد من الوجه الواحد على الطريقة الواحدة فيلزمهم أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى مثلا لعالمية واحد منا وتلزمهم المحالات المذكورة. الرابع : هب أنه يلزم أن يكون علم الله تعالى مثلا لعلم الواحد منا ، لكن لم يلزم من هذا القدر التساوي في القدم والحدوث؟ أليس أن الوجود في والغائب له حقيقة واحد ومعقول واحد مع أن الوجود في الشاهد متجدد، وفي الغائب دائم ؟ فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في المعلوم؟
As for the first doubt: the answer to it is in several aspects: First: Why is it not permissible to say: two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and in one manner are equal in this relation, and this relation is a necessary concomitant of them, and it has been established that things different in quiddity are not impossible to share in the intellect in one concomitant, and if that is so, then the equality of the two knowledges in this measure does not necessitate their similarity? If they say: if this measure does not necessitate the similarity of the two knowledges, then by which way do you know the similarity of the two knowledges in the witnessed realm? We say: we have shown that this measure does not require certainty of similarity, and if in the witnessed realm no other way is obtained except this way, then it is necessary not to be certain about similarity in the witnessed realm either, but to suspend judgment, and if another way besides this way is obtained, we are certain by it, and this is a decisive answer. Second: Why is it not permissible to say: knowledge in the witnessed realm is not related to the known; rather, what is related to the known is the property of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya)? If knowledge is not related to the known, then their statement falls, that two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and one manner necessitate the statement that they are similar. Third: What they impose upon us regarding the two knowledges is imposed upon them regarding the two properties of being knowing. For the property of being knowing of God exalted and our property of being knowing have been related to the same known in the same aspect and the same manner, so it would be necessary for them that the property of being knowing of God be similar to the property of being knowing of one of us, and the mentioned impossible consequences follow. Fourth: Grant that it is necessary that God's knowledge be similar to the knowledge of one of us, but does this measure necessitate equality in preeternity and temporal origination? Is it not that existence in the witnessed realm and the absent realm has one reality and one intelligible, while existence in the witnessed realm is temporal and in the absent realm is perpetual? So why is it not permissible that the matter be thus in the known?
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a response to the first doubt raised by those who deny God's knowledge. Imam Razi provides four answers. The first answer argues that two knowledges relating to the same known in every respect are equal only in that relation, but this does not entail similarity (tamāthul) because different essences can share a necessary concomitant. If the opponent objects that in the witnessed realm we only have this relation to judge similarity, Razi replies that we should then suspend judgment unless another criterion is found. The second answer distinguishes between knowledge itself (al-ʿilm) and the quality of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya): in the witnessed realm, knowledge may not be directly related to the known; rather, the relation is through the attribute of being knowing, so the argument collapses. The third answer turns the tables: the same reasoning applied to knowledge would apply to the attribute of being knowing, forcing the opponent to accept that God's attribute of being knowing is similar to ours—an absurd consequence. The fourth answer grants for argument's sake that God's knowledge is similar to ours in quiddity, but this similarity does not imply equality in preeternity and temporality, just as existence is one in reality but differs in perpetuity and temporality.

Key Terms
علم
تماثل
لازم
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if two things share a property (like relating to the same object), they must be similar in essence.
Correction: Razi shows that different essences can share a necessary concomitant, so shared relation does not prove essential similarity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first answer, why does equality in the relation of two knowledges to one known not necessitate their similarity?
A) Because the relation is not real.
B) Because different essences can share a necessary concomitant.
C) Because knowledge is not an accident.
D) Because God's knowledge is incomparable.

Key Takeaway
Equality in a concomitant does not prove essential similarity; the first answer blocks the inference from shared relation to similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge, do you instinctively imagine it as similar to your own knowledge but just 'more'? This lesson shows that even the same relation to a known does not imply similarity. In what specific area of your life have you unconsciously compared Allah to creation? Will you actively correct that thought today by reminding yourself of His absolute transcendence?
?
Razi says that if the only way to know similarity is through shared relation, we must suspend judgment. Are there attributes of Allah that you have claimed to understand fully, based only on analogy from yourself? Which attribute, and what step will you take to instead affirm it as He has described without resemblance?
?
The passage calls Razi's answer 'decisive'. Do you feel that same confidence in rational arguments for tawheed? If not, what is one rational proof about Allah's attributes that you have not internalized? Commit to studying that proof until you can explain it to someone else.

Second Answer: Distinguishing Knowledge from the Property of Being Knowing
Second answer to the first doubt about God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that knowledge itself (al-ʿilm) is not directly related to the known; rather, the property of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) is what relates, thereby breaking the assumed similarity.

Core Idea
Knowledge relates indirectly; the direct relation is through the attribute of being knowing.

Imam Razi's second answer introduces a subtle distinction. He argues that in the witnessed realm, knowledge (al-ʿilm) is not directly related to the known object; rather, the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) is what bears the relation. If the relation is not directly between knowledge and the known, then the opponent's argument that two knowledges relate in the same way collapses. This distinction preserves the possibility that God's knowledge may be fundamentally different.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Razi makes a distinction between 'ilm and 'ālimiyya.
2
Understand the distinction
In the witnessed realm, a knower's knowledge is an inherent quality, but the relation to the known is through the attribute of being knowing (the state of being a knower). Knowledge itself may not be directly related.
This is a metaphysical point about the nature of knowledge.
3
Apply to the doubt
If the relation is not directly between two knowledges but between two properties of being knowing, then the original argument about two knowledges is invalid.
4
Reflect on the implication
This answer defends God's transcendence by showing that the analogical reasoning from the witnessed realm fails because the terms of comparison are not the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الأولى : فالجواب عنها بوجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد و على طريقة واحدة فإنهما متساويان في هذا التعلق، وهذا التعلق من لوازمهما، وقد ثبت أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في العقل في لازم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من استواء العلمين في هذا القدر تماثلهما ؟ فإن قالوا : فإذا لم يلزم من هذا القدر تماثل العلمين فبأي طريق تعرفون تماثل العلمين في الشاهد؟ فنقول : قد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يقتضي الجزم بالتماثل، وإن لم يحصل في الشاهد إلا هذا الطريق وجب أن لا يقطع في الشاهد أيضا بالتماثل بل يتوقف فيه، وإن حصل فيه طريق آخر سوى هذا الطريق قطعنا به وهذا جواب قاطع. الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم في الشاهد غير متعلق بالمعلوم، إنما المتعلق بالمعلوم هو العالمية وإذا لم يكن العلم متعلقا بالمعلوم بطل قولهم العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة يوجب القول بكونهما متماثلين. الثالث : أن ما ألزموه علينا في العلمين يلزمهم في العالميتين، فإن عالمية الله تعالى وعالميتنا قد تعلقتا بالمعلوم الواحد من الوجه الواحد على الطريقة الواحدة فيلزمهم أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى مثلا لعالمية واحد منا وتلزمهم المحالات المذكورة. الرابع : هب أنه يلزم أن يكون علم الله تعالى مثلا لعلم الواحد منا ، لكن لم يلزم من هذا القدر التساوي في القدم والحدوث؟ أليس أن الوجود في والغائب له حقيقة واحد ومعقول واحد مع أن الوجود في الشاهد متجدد، وفي الغائب دائم ؟ فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في المعلوم؟
As for the first doubt: the answer to it is in several aspects: First: Why is it not permissible to say: two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and in one manner are equal in this relation, and this relation is a necessary concomitant of them, and it has been established that things different in quiddity are not impossible to share in the intellect in one concomitant, and if that is so, then the equality of the two knowledges in this measure does not necessitate their similarity? If they say: if this measure does not necessitate the similarity of the two knowledges, then by which way do you know the similarity of the two knowledges in the witnessed realm? We say: we have shown that this measure does not require certainty of similarity, and if in the witnessed realm no other way is obtained except this way, then it is necessary not to be certain about similarity in the witnessed realm either, but to suspend judgment, and if another way besides this way is obtained, we are certain by it, and this is a decisive answer. Second: Why is it not permissible to say: knowledge in the witnessed realm is not related to the known; rather, what is related to the known is the property of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya)? If knowledge is not related to the known, then their statement falls, that two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and one manner necessitate the statement that they are similar. Third: What they impose upon us regarding the two knowledges is imposed upon them regarding the two properties of being knowing. For the property of being knowing of God exalted and our property of being knowing have been related to the same known in the same aspect and the same manner, so it would be necessary for them that the property of being knowing of God be similar to the property of being knowing of one of us, and the mentioned impossible consequences follow. Fourth: Grant that it is necessary that God's knowledge be similar to the knowledge of one of us, but does this measure necessitate equality in preeternity and temporal origination? Is it not that existence in the witnessed realm and the absent realm has one reality and one intelligible, while existence in the witnessed realm is temporal and in the absent realm is perpetual? So why is it not permissible that the matter be thus in the known?
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a response to the first doubt raised by those who deny God's knowledge. Imam Razi provides four answers. The first answer argues that two knowledges relating to the same known in every respect are equal only in that relation, but this does not entail similarity (tamāthul) because different essences can share a necessary concomitant. If the opponent objects that in the witnessed realm we only have this relation to judge similarity, Razi replies that we should then suspend judgment unless another criterion is found. The second answer distinguishes between knowledge itself (al-ʿilm) and the quality of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya): in the witnessed realm, knowledge may not be directly related to the known; rather, the relation is through the attribute of being knowing, so the argument collapses. The third answer turns the tables: the same reasoning applied to knowledge would apply to the attribute of being knowing, forcing the opponent to accept that God's attribute of being knowing is similar to ours—an absurd consequence. The fourth answer grants for argument's sake that God's knowledge is similar to ours in quiddity, but this similarity does not imply equality in preeternity and temporality, just as existence is one in reality but differs in perpetuity and temporality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that knowledge itself is what relates directly to the known.
Correction: Razi suggests it is the property of being knowing, not knowledge as a discrete entity, that bears the relation.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim is directly related to the known in the witnessed realm?
A) Knowledge itself
B) The property of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya)
C) The intellect
D) The soul

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not directly related; the direct relation is through the attribute of being knowing, which breaks the analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as similar to human knowledge—limited, sequential, and tied to objects? How does Razi's distinction between knowledge and the attribute of being knowing challenge you to purge such human-like assumptions from your belief? Will you actively remind yourself that God's knowledge is not a relation to things but He is simply knowing in a way that befits His majesty?
?
This lesson separates 'knowledge' from 'the property of being knowing' to protect God's transcendence. Have you ever considered that even our conceptual categories (like 'attribute') might fall short of capturing the reality of the Divine? What will you do to cultivate a deeper humility before the mystery of God's attributes, rather than claiming full comprehension?
?
If the direct relation to creation is through God's attribute of being knowing, not His knowledge itself, how does that reshape your understanding of His intimacy with your every thought and deed? Are you comforted that His awareness of you is not mediated by a faculty that could be analogous to yours? Will you thank Him for being the All-Knowing in a way that no created mind can grasp?

Third Answer: Turning the Tables on the Opponent
Third answer to the first doubt about God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third argument that the opponent's reasoning about knowledge applies equally to the attribute of being knowing, leading to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
The opponent's logic backfires on their own view of divine attributes.

In his third answer, Imam Razi uses a tactical move: he applies the same reasoning that the opponent used against God's knowledge to the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya). If the opponent's logic were sound, then God's attribute of being knowing would have to be similar to our attribute of being knowing, which is absurd. This shows the flaw in the opponent's original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the third answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Razi mirrors the opponent's argument.
2
Identify the mirrored argument
The opponent claimed that two knowledges relating to the same known must be similar. Razi now says: by the same token, two attributes of being knowing relating to the same known must be similar.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
If God's attribute of being knowing were similar to ours, that would involve impossible consequences (e.g., it would be contingent like ours, etc.).
The text mentions 'the mentioned impossible consequences'.
4
Appreciate the rhetorical force
This answer does not prove God's knowledge is different, but it exposes a contradiction in the opponent's position: they cannot accept the implication for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الأولى : فالجواب عنها بوجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد و على طريقة واحدة فإنهما متساويان في هذا التعلق، وهذا التعلق من لوازمهما، وقد ثبت أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية لا يمتنع اشتراكها في العقل في لازم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من استواء العلمين في هذا القدر تماثلهما ؟ فإن قالوا : فإذا لم يلزم من هذا القدر تماثل العلمين فبأي طريق تعرفون تماثل العلمين في الشاهد؟ فنقول : قد بينا أن هذا القدر لا يقتضي الجزم بالتماثل، وإن لم يحصل في الشاهد إلا هذا الطريق وجب أن لا يقطع في الشاهد أيضا بالتماثل بل يتوقف فيه، وإن حصل فيه طريق آخر سوى هذا الطريق قطعنا به وهذا جواب قاطع. الثاني: لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم في الشاهد غير متعلق بالمعلوم، إنما المتعلق بالمعلوم هو العالمية وإذا لم يكن العلم متعلقا بالمعلوم بطل قولهم العلمان المتعلقان بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد على طريقة واحدة يوجب القول بكونهما متماثلين. الثالث : أن ما ألزموه علينا في العلمين يلزمهم في العالميتين، فإن عالمية الله تعالى وعالميتنا قد تعلقتا بالمعلوم الواحد من الوجه الواحد على الطريقة الواحدة فيلزمهم أن تكون عالمية الله تعالى مثلا لعالمية واحد منا وتلزمهم المحالات المذكورة. الرابع : هب أنه يلزم أن يكون علم الله تعالى مثلا لعلم الواحد منا ، لكن لم يلزم من هذا القدر التساوي في القدم والحدوث؟ أليس أن الوجود في والغائب له حقيقة واحد ومعقول واحد مع أن الوجود في الشاهد متجدد، وفي الغائب دائم ؟ فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في المعلوم؟
As for the first doubt: the answer to it is in several aspects: First: Why is it not permissible to say: two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and in one manner are equal in this relation, and this relation is a necessary concomitant of them, and it has been established that things different in quiddity are not impossible to share in the intellect in one concomitant, and if that is so, then the equality of the two knowledges in this measure does not necessitate their similarity? If they say: if this measure does not necessitate the similarity of the two knowledges, then by which way do you know the similarity of the two knowledges in the witnessed realm? We say: we have shown that this measure does not require certainty of similarity, and if in the witnessed realm no other way is obtained except this way, then it is necessary not to be certain about similarity in the witnessed realm either, but to suspend judgment, and if another way besides this way is obtained, we are certain by it, and this is a decisive answer. Second: Why is it not permissible to say: knowledge in the witnessed realm is not related to the known; rather, what is related to the known is the property of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya)? If knowledge is not related to the known, then their statement falls, that two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and one manner necessitate the statement that they are similar. Third: What they impose upon us regarding the two knowledges is imposed upon them regarding the two properties of being knowing. For the property of being knowing of God exalted and our property of being knowing have been related to the same known in the same aspect and the same manner, so it would be necessary for them that the property of being knowing of God be similar to the property of being knowing of one of us, and the mentioned impossible consequences follow. Fourth: Grant that it is necessary that God's knowledge be similar to the knowledge of one of us, but does this measure necessitate equality in preeternity and temporal origination? Is it not that existence in the witnessed realm and the absent realm has one reality and one intelligible, while existence in the witnessed realm is temporal and in the absent realm is perpetual? So why is it not permissible that the matter be thus in the known?
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a response to the first doubt raised by those who deny God's knowledge. Imam Razi provides four answers. The first answer argues that two knowledges relating to the same known in every respect are equal only in that relation, but this does not entail similarity (tamāthul) because different essences can share a necessary concomitant. If the opponent objects that in the witnessed realm we only have this relation to judge similarity, Razi replies that we should then suspend judgment unless another criterion is found. The second answer distinguishes between knowledge itself (al-ʿilm) and the quality of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya): in the witnessed realm, knowledge may not be directly related to the known; rather, the relation is through the attribute of being knowing, so the argument collapses. The third answer turns the tables: the same reasoning applied to knowledge would apply to the attribute of being knowing, forcing the opponent to accept that God's attribute of being knowing is similar to ours—an absurd consequence. The fourth answer grants for argument's sake that God's knowledge is similar to ours in quiddity, but this similarity does not imply equality in preeternity and temporality, just as existence is one in reality but differs in perpetuity and temporality.

Key Terms
عالمية
تماثل
شاهد
غائب
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think that only knowledge itself is relevant, not the attribute of being knowing.
Correction: Razi shows that the same logic applies to the attribute, causing trouble for the opponent.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue in the third answer?
A) God's knowledge is completely different from ours.
B) The opponent's reasoning about knowledge would force them to say God's attribute of being knowing is similar to ours.
C) Knowledge and the attribute of being knowing are the same.
D) The doubt is invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's own logic, when applied consistently, leads to absurd conclusions about divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge and your own knowledge, is there a part of you that secretly assumes they are basically the same kind of thing? What does this argument reveal about that assumption?
?
Have you ever used logic to defend a belief, only to realize that the same logic could be used against you? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own arguments more honestly before God?
?
This argument shows that applying human standards to God leads to absurdity. In what area of your life are you still expecting God to act according to human logic? What would it take for you to truly submit to the fact that His attributes are beyond comparison?

Fourth Answer: Similarity in Quiddity Does Not Imply Equality in Temporal Status
Fourth answer to the first doubt about God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth argument that even if God's knowledge were similar in quiddity to human knowledge, it does not follow that they share the same temporal properties (preeternity vs. temporality).

Core Idea
Similarity in essence does not entail equality in modality (preeternity/temporality).

Imam Razi's fourth answer grants the opponent's claim for the sake of argument: suppose God's knowledge is similar in essence to human knowledge. Even then, this similarity does not force us to say that God's knowledge is temporal like ours. Razi points to the example of existence: existence in the witnessed realm is temporal, but existence in the absent (divine) realm is perpetual, yet they share the same quiddity. Similarly, knowledge could have one quiddity but differ in being preeternal or temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Razi uses an analogy with existence.
2
Understand the analogy
Existence has one reality but manifests differently: temporal in the witnessed realm, eternal in the absent realm. Similarly, knowledge could be one in essence but differ in preeternity.
3
Apply to the doubt
Even if God's knowledge is like ours in quiddity, it does not have to be temporal; it can be preeternal.
4
Recognize the concession strategy
This answer shows that Razi can afford to grant similarity in essence without conceding the opponent's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the first doubt: the answer to it is in several aspects: First: Why is it not permissible to say: two knowledges related to one known in one aspect and in one manner are equal in this relation, and this relation is a necessary concomitant of them, and it has been established that things different in quiddity are not impossible to share in the intellect in one concomitant, and if that is so, then the equality of the two knowledges in this measure does not necessitate their similarity? If they say: if this measure does not necessitate the similarity of the two knowledges, then by which way do you know the similarity of the two knowledges in the witnessed realm? We say: we have shown that this measure does not require certainty of similarity, and if in the witnessed realm no other way is obtained except this way, then it is necessary not to be certain about similarity in the witnessed realm either, but to suspend judgment, and if another way besides this way is obtained, we are certain by it, and this is a decisive answer. ?

?

Second Doubt on God's Knowledge and Its Refutation
Imam Razi refutes the second doubt and demonstrates that one knowledge can relate to multiple knowns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument that infinite knowns require infinite knowledges by demonstrating that one knowledge can relate to many knowns.

Core Idea
One knowledge can encompass many objects, as shown by the relational example of blackness and whiteness.

Imam Razi addresses the second doubt against God's knowledge: how can one knowledge cover infinite knowns? The opponent presents a trilemma, but Razi turns the argument back on them and provides a positive proof using the relation of opposites. This lesson traces his argument and its philosophical implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
Read the opening of the passage to grasp the opponent's trilemma: either God knows infinite things by one knowledge, by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges. Each option is problematic for the opponent's own position.
Notice how Razi immediately turns the question back on the opponent regarding the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya).
2
See Razi's initial response
Observe that Razi says the same doubt applies to the opponent's own view about the attribute of being knowing. This shifts the burden back.
3
Examine the proof
Focus on the key example: knowledge that blackness is opposite of whiteness. This knowledge necessarily relates to both blackness and whiteness, showing that one knowledge can relate to many knowns.
This is a concrete demonstration that one knowledge can have multiple objects.
4
Consider the objection and reply
The opponent might object that if two knowns can be known separately (one while heedless of the other), they cannot be known by one knowledge. Razi replies that in the example, blackness is known while heedless of whiteness, yet the relational knowledge covers both simultaneously.
This refutes the objection and solidifies the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : - وهي قولهم: إما أن يعلم المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها بعلم واحد أو بعلوم متناهية أو بعلوم غير متناهية - فنقول : هذه الشبهة بتمامها واردة عليكم في العالمية، وكل ما تقولونه في العالمية نقوله في العلم. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يعلم جميع المعلومات بعلم واحد؟ وما ذكرتموه من الوجوه في بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يعلم بالعلم الواحد إلا معلوما واحدا فهو معارض من دليل آخر وهو أن العلم المتعلق بكون السواد ضد البياض مثلا لا بد أن يكون ذلك العلم بعينه متعلقا بالبياض والسواد لأنه إذا لم يكن ذلك العلم المتعلق متعلقا بالسواد والبياض فحينئذ لا يكون متعلقا بالمضادة بين السواد والبياض، بل أقصى ما في الباب أنه يكون متعلقا بالمضادة ونحن لا نلزم الكلام في العلم المتعلق بالمضادة وإنما تلزم الكلام في العلم المتعلق بمضادة السواد والبياض. لا يقال : هب أنه حصل من هذا الدليل أن كل معلومين يجوز أن يعلم أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر فإنه يجوز أن يُعْلَمَا بعلم واحد لأنا نقول : لما ثبت أن العلم المتعلق بمضادة السواد والبياض متعلق بالسواد والبياض معا فلا شك أن السواد يجوز أن يعلم حال الذهول عن البياض، فلما صار السواد والبياض معلومين بهذا العلم الواحد حصل أن المعلومين الذين يجوز أن يعلم أحدهما مع الذهول عن الآخر يمتنع أن يعلما بعلم واحد فثبت بهذا الدليل أنه لا يمتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بالمعلومات الكثيرة.
As for the second doubt—which is their statement: either He knows infinite known things by one knowledge, or by finite knowledges, or by infinite knowledges—we say: this doubt entirely applies to you regarding the property of being knowing, and everything you say about the property of being knowing we say about knowledge. Then we say: why is it not permissible that all known things be known by one knowledge? And whatever aspects you have mentioned in showing that only one known can be known by one knowledge is contradicted by another proof: that knowledge related to, for example, blackness being opposite of whiteness necessarily is that same knowledge related to whiteness and blackness, because if that knowledge is not related to blackness and whiteness, then it would not be related to the opposition between blackness and whiteness; rather, at most it would be related to opposition in general, and we are not concerned with knowledge related to opposition in general, but only with knowledge related to the opposition of blackness and whiteness. It may not be said: grant that from this proof it follows that any two knowns may be known such that one can be known while heedless of the other, then it is permissible that they be known by one knowledge. For we say: since it is established that knowledge related to the opposition of blackness and whiteness is related to blackness and whiteness together, there is no doubt that blackness may be known while heedless of whiteness; so since blackness and whiteness become known by this one knowledge, it follows that two knowns such that one may be known while heedless of the other cannot be known by one knowledge. So it is established by this proof that it is not impossible for one knowledge to relate to many knowns.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage answers the second doubt, which poses a trilemma: if God knows infinite things, either by one knowledge, finite knowledges, or infinite knowledges (each problematic). Razi first notes that the same argument applies to the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya), so the opponent is equally stuck. Then he provides a positive proof that one knowledge can relate to many knowns: the knowledge that blackness is opposite of whiteness necessarily relates to both blackness and whiteness; otherwise it would only relate to opposition in general, not the specific opposition. From this example, the opponent might object that if two knowns can be known separately (one while heedless of the other), they cannot be known by one knowledge. But Razi replies that in the example, blackness can be known while heedless of whiteness (e.g., knowing blackness alone), yet the same knowledge of opposition relates to both simultaneously. Thus it is possible for one knowledge to encompass many knowns.

Key Terms
علم
عالمية
معلوم
مضادة
سواد
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One knowledge can only know one object.
Correction: Razi shows that knowledge of a relation (like opposition) necessarily relates to multiple objects, so one knowledge can encompass many knowns.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to show that one knowledge can relate to many knowns?
A) Knowledge of God's essence.
B) Knowledge that blackness is opposite of whiteness.
C) Knowledge of the soul.
D) Knowledge of past and future.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that one knowledge can relate to multiple objects, using the relational knowledge of opposites as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's knowledge encompasses even the relationships between things, like the opposition between blackness and whiteness, do you truly believe that He knows every detail of your life, including the connections you cannot see? How does that affect your trust in Him today?
?
Razi argued that one knowledge can cover many objects. If God's knowledge is one yet covers all, what does that mean for your understanding of His omniscience? Does it comfort you or challenge you to live more consciously before Him?
?
Think of a sin or a blessing in your life that you consider isolated from everything else. How does the fact that God's knowledge connects all things (like blackness to whiteness) change how you view that event? Will you turn to Him with that awareness now?

Third Doubt on God's Knowledge Answered
Imam Razi answers the third doubt on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's response to the doubt about God's knowledge of His own knowledge, including the counter-argument and the possibility of self-relation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of Himself is not a special problem; the same issue applies to any attribute.

This lesson addresses the third doubt raised by opponents: if God knows His own knowledge, must it be by the same knowledge or another? Both options seem problematic. Imam Razi offers two responses: first, the same problem applies to the attribute of being knowing (ʿālimiyya); second, knowledge can relate to itself, leading to a chain of relations that is acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
Read the passage and identify the trilemma: either God knows His knowledge by itself or by another knowledge. Both are presented as problematic by the opponent.
The doubt is about infinite regress or circularity.
2
See Razi's first response
Razi points out that the same doubt applies to the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya). So the opponent cannot use it as a special objection against God's knowledge.
This is a tu quoque argument.
3
See Razi's second response
Razi suggests that knowledge can relate to itself. If so, there would be multiple ordered relations (e.g., knowledge of knowledge of knowledge...). This infinite regress is not necessarily problematic for divine attributes.
The opponent must also accept such a chain for their own attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : - وهي قولهم : إما أن يعلم علمه بنفسه أو بعلم آخر ـ قلنا : هذا وارد عليكم في العالمية أيضًا سواء بسواء، وأيضًا فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم متعلقا بنفسه؟ ثم ثانيًا بذلك التعلق فيكون هناك تعلقات مترتبة كثيرة ولا بد لكم من التزام مثل هذا الكلام في العالمية.
As for the third doubt—which is their statement: either He knows His knowledge by itself or by another knowledge—we say: this applies to you regarding the property of being knowing as well, equally. Also, why is it not permissible that knowledge be related to itself? Then secondly, by that relation, there would be many ordered relations, and you must commit to such a statement regarding the property of being knowing.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is the third doubt: if God knows His own knowledge, does He know it by that same knowledge or by another? Either option seems problematic. Razi first turns the tables: the same question applies to the opponent's own concept of the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya). Then he suggests that knowledge can relate to itself; if so, multiple ordered relations follow (e.g., knowledge of knowledge of knowledge...). This infinite regress is not necessarily problematic for divine attributes, as the opponent would also have to accept it for their own attribute.

Key Terms
علم
عالمية
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress in divine attributes is always invalid and cannot be allowed.
Correction: Razi shows that an infinite regress of relations (e.g., knowledge of knowledge) is not necessarily problematic for God, and the opponent's own position leads to the same regress.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's first response to the third doubt?
A) He denies that God knows His own knowledge.
B) He argues the doubt applies equally to the opponent's attribute of being knowing.
C) He claims knowledge cannot be related to itself.
D) He asserts that God does not need to know His own knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends God's knowledge by showing the opponent's own position leads to the same difficulty, and by allowing self-relation of knowledge with an infinite regress that is acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every single thought you have, including the ones you are ashamed of? How would your actions today change if you lived with that certainty?
?
When you object to God's attributes because they seem logically problematic, are you willing to apply the same scrutiny to your own assumptions? What doubt about God do you need to surrender to His wisdom?
?
Razi shows that infinite regress is not always a problem. Is there a limit to your trust in God's knowledge? In what area of your life do you act as if God does not know what is best for you?

Countering the Fourth Doubt About God's Knowledge
Razi counters the fourth doubt with four Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends God's knowledge by countering a verse used to deny it with four explicit Quranic verses that affirm His knowledge.

Core Idea
A single ambiguous verse does not override multiple clear ones.

Imam Razi now addresses the fourth doubt, which is based on a Quranic verse that might be interpreted to limit God's knowledge. He shows that this interpretation is untenable because other verses clearly affirm God's knowledge. Let's see how he resolves the apparent conflict using the principle of countering ambiguous verses with clear ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and its basis
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the fourth doubt: the verse 'And above every knower is the All-Knowing'.
Notice that the doubters use this verse to question God's knowledge.
2
Identify the counter-answer
Razi's answer is that this verse is countered by four other verses that explicitly affirm God's knowledge.
This is a common method in kalam: reconciling ambiguous verses with clear ones.
3
List the four counter-verses
Find each of the four verses Razi cites: 4:166, 35:11, 2:255, 31:34. Understand what each affirms.
These verses clearly state that God's knowledge encompasses everything.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this answer is effective: clear verses override ambiguous ones. This protects the concept of God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة . وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦] - فجوابها أنها معارضة بالآيات الدالة على إثبات العلم، وهي أربعة : قوله تعالى : ﴿ أَنزَلَهُ بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [النساء : ١٦٦] ، ﴿ وَمَا تَحْمِلُ مِنْ أُنثَى وَلَا تَضَعُ إِلَّا بِعِلْمِهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۱]، ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٥٥]، ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ عِندَهُ عِلْمُ السَّاعَةِ ﴾ [لقمان: ٣٤] .
As for the fourth doubt—which is clinging to His saying, 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' (Quran 12:76)—the answer is that it is countered by verses indicating the affirmation of knowledge, which are four: His saying, 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166); 'No female conceives nor gives birth except with His knowledge' (35:11); 'They encompass not anything of His knowledge' (2:255); 'Indeed, Allah, with Him is knowledge of the Hour' (31:34).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is the fourth doubt: a literal reading of Quran 12:76 ('Above every knower is the All-Knowing') might suggest that there are knowers above God, or that God is only the highest knower but not all-knowing. Alternatively, the verse could be used to deny that God has knowledge (since He is above all knowers, perhaps He does not have knowledge like ours). Razi answers that this verse is countered by four other clear verses that explicitly affirm God's knowledge: He sent down the Quran with His knowledge; no female conceives or gives birth except with His knowledge; they encompass nothing of His knowledge; and He alone has knowledge of the Hour. These verses unambiguously establish that God has knowledge, so any interpretation of the first verse that denies knowledge is rejected.

آيات
معارضة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'above every knower' means God is not a Knower.
Correction: Razi shows that this interpretation is outweighed by explicit verses affirming His knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the fourth doubt?
A) He argues that the verse is weak.
B) He counters it with four other Qur'anic verses proving God's knowledge.
C) He interprets the verse metaphorically.
D) He says the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the fourth doubt by showing that clear Quranic verses affirming God's knowledge take precedence over an ambiguous one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When doubts about God's attributes arise, do you turn to the clear verses or let the doubt linger? What concrete step will you take today to strengthen your certainty in Allah's knowledge?
