Scriptural Proof for God's Hearing and Sight
Razi presents Qur'anic verses and the principle of relying on apparent meanings unless a condition is proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural basis for affirming God's attributes of hearing and sight and the methodological principle of relying on apparent meanings unless a contrary proof is provided.

Core Idea
Qur'anic verses settle divine attributes unless impossibility is proven.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi turns from rational proofs to scriptural evidence for God's hearing and sight. He cites key verses and establishes a crucial principle: the apparent meanings of the Qur'an are authoritative unless an opponent can demonstrate a condition that makes them impossible for God. This lesson unpacks that argument and its methodological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Pay attention to the flow: first the claim, then the verses, then the principle.
Notice the phrase 'إلا أن يقيم الخصم دلالة' which introduces the exception.
2
Identify the verses
Locate the three verses cited. What do they have in common? They all affirm or imply that God hears and sees.
The verses are from Surah Taha, Maryam, and al-An'am.
3
Understand the methodological principle
Razi states that many texts necessitate affirming these attributes unless the opponent proves a condition that makes them impossible. This principle governs how to handle apparent meanings.
The burden of proof is on the one claiming ta'wil (interpretation).
4
Take the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question about the default stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في بيان أنه تعالى موصوف بالسمع والبصر. والدليل عليه هو أن السمع والبصر من صفات الكمال وضدهما من النقصان والقرآن وارد بإثباتهما لله تعالى قال الله تعالى : {إنني معكما أسمع وأرى} [طه: ٤٦] وقال إبراهيم لأبيه : {يَاأَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ} [مريم: ٤٢] وقال الله تعالى: {لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ} [الأنعام: ١٠٣] وإذا كانت النصوص الكثيرة واردة بذلك وجب إثباتها لله تعالى، إلا أن يقيم الخصم دلالة على أن ثبوت هاتين الحالتين مشروط بشرط ممتنع التحقق في حق الله تعالى، فيجب المصير إلى التأويل، وتكون الدلالة إشارة إلى المعارضة، ومن ادعاها كان عليه البيان. هذا هو الذي يعول عليه في هذا الباب.
Chapter Two: On proving that He, exalted, is described with hearing and sight. The proof for it is that hearing and sight are attributes of perfection and their opposites are of deficiency, and the Qur'an has come establishing them for God, exalted. God said: {Indeed I am with you, I hear and see} (20:46). And Abraham said to his father: {O my father, why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see?} (19:42). And God said: {Sights do not perceive Him, and He perceives sights} (6:103). And when many texts have come with that, it is necessary to affirm them for God, except if the opponent brings a proof that the establishment of these two states is conditioned by a condition whose realization is impossible with respect to God, then one must resort to interpretation (ta'wil), and that proof would be a reference to opposition (mu'arada), and whoever claims it must provide the explanation. This is what is relied upon in this chapter.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a scriptural proof for affirming the attributes of hearing and sight for God. It begins by stating that these are perfections and their opposites are deficiencies. Then it cites three Qur'anic verses: God telling Moses and Aaron He hears and sees, Abraham rebuking his father for worshiping idols that cannot hear or see, and the verse that sights do not comprehend God but He comprehends them. These texts necessitate affirming these attributes. The principle is that one holds to the apparent meaning unless the opponent can prove a condition that would make them impossible for God, in which case interpretation (ta'wil) would be necessary. The burden of proof is on the one claiming such a condition. This is the foundational approach.

Key Terms
صفات الكمال
النقصان
النصوص
التأويل
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpretation (ta'wil) is the default approach to divine attributes.
Correction: The default is to affirm the apparent meaning unless a proof of impossibility is brought.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the default stance regarding the Qur'anic verses that affirm God's hearing and sight?
A) They must be interpreted allegorically.
B) They are taken at face value unless a proof against them is provided.
C) They only apply to prophets.
D) They are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The apparent meanings of Qur'anic verses affirming divine attributes are accepted unless a proof of impossibility is provided.

Proof and Critique of God's Hearing and Sight
Imam Razi presents the majority's proof and critiques its first premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the majority used to establish God's hearing and sight, and Imam Razi's critique of its first premise.

Core Idea
Rational proof must account for God's uniqueness, or it fails.

The majority of theologians argued that because God is alive, He must possess either hearing and sight or their opposites; since opposites are defects, He must have hearing and sight. Imam Razi then subjects this argument to scrutiny, pointing out that its first premise—that every living being can have these attributes—does not necessarily apply to God, whose essence is unique. This lesson unpacks both the proof and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author’s own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premises, dichotomy, elimination.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Break down the proof: major premise (God is alive), minor premise (every living being can have hearing/sight), dichotomy (either hearing/sight or opposites), elimination (opposites are defects, impossible for God). Conclusion: God has hearing and sight.
The proof relies on an analogy between God and other living beings.
3
Examine the critique
Imam Razi challenges the first premise. He notes that other living beings can have ignorance, desire, etc., which are impossible for God. Thus, the premise does not hold universally.
The key insight: God's essence and life are unique.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Does this critique reject the attributes themselves? No, it only shows the rational proof is not airtight. Scriptural proofs remain.
This highlights the need for careful reasoning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج جمهور الأصحاب بأنه تعالى حي، وكل حي يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، وكل من صح أن يكون موصوفًا بصفة وجب أن يكون موصوفا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها ، وضد السمع والبصر هو الصمم والعمى، فثبت أنه لا بد وأن يكون الباري تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، أو بالصمم والعمى، وهذان الوصفان من باب النقائص والآفات، وهي على الله تعالى محال، ولما امتنع كونه تعالى موصوفًا بأضداد السمع والبصر وجب كونه تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر وهو المطلوب. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات يعسر تقريرها : أما المقدمة الأولى فهي قوله : كل حي يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، فنقول : أليس كل حي يصح في المشاهد أن يكون موصوفا بالجهل والظن والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة، ثم إنه تعالى حي مع أنه لا يصح عليه شيء من ذلك؟ فعلمنا أنه لا يلزم من كونه حيا أن يصح عليه ما يصح على سائر الأحياء. وتقرير القول فيه : أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات، وحياته تعالى مخالفة لحياة سائر الأحياء، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من صحة السمع والبصر على سائر الأحياء صحتها على ذاته وحياته.
The majority of the companions (ashab) argued that He, exalted, is alive (hayy), and every living being can possibly be described with hearing and sight, and whoever can possibly be described with an attribute must necessarily be described with that attribute or its opposite. The opposite of hearing and sight is deafness and blindness. So it is established that the Creator must necessarily be described with hearing and sight or with deafness and blindness. These two descriptions are of the category of deficiencies and defects, and they are impossible for God. Since it is impossible for Him to be described with the opposites of hearing and sight, it is necessary that He be described with hearing and sight, which is the desired conclusion. Know that this proof is built upon premises whose exposition is difficult: As for the first premise, which is his statement: 'Every living being can possibly be described with hearing and sight,' we say: Is it not the case that every living being in the observable world can possibly be described with ignorance, suspicion, desire, aversion, pain, and pleasure, and then He, exalted, is alive yet none of these are possible for Him? So we know that from His being alive it does not necessitate that whatever is possible for other living beings is possible for Him. The clarification of the statement in it is: His essence is contrary to other essences, and His life is contrary to the life of other living beings. If this is so, then the possibility of hearing and sight for other living beings does not necessitate their possibility for His essence and His life.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof by the majority of scholars for God's hearing and sight, followed by a critique of its first premise. The proof uses the fact that God is alive, and every living being can have hearing/sight, leading to a dichotomy: either He has them or their opposites, but the opposites are impossible, so He must have them. Imam Razi then notes that this proof relies on premises that are difficult to establish. The first premise claims that every living being can have hearing/sight, but the author points out that many attributes (like ignorance, desire) are possible for other living beings but not for God. Since God's essence and life are unique, the possibility of hearing/sight for other living beings does not automatically extend to God. Thus, the first premise is not universally valid.

Key Terms
حي
الصمم والعمى
النقائص والآفات
الذات
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the critique disproves God's attributes of hearing and sight.
Correction: The critique only shows the rational proof is not airtight; the attributes are still established by scripture and other proofs.

Checkpoint
What weakness does Imam Razi identify in the first premise of the rational proof?
A) The premise is not found in the Qur'an.
B) Other living beings can have attributes that God cannot have, so the premise is not universally true.
C) The proof uses circular reasoning.
D) Hearing and sight are not perfections.

Key Takeaway
Rational proofs for divine attributes must consider God's absolute uniqueness, as what applies to creation does not automatically apply to the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God is alive, do you unconsciously impose limitations from your own experience of life? What specific thought pattern must you correct to honor His absolute uniqueness?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly logical proof for hearing and sight can fail if it does not account for God's transcendence. In what ways have you trusted your own reasoning about God without checking it against His revealed attributes?
?
If God's essence is 'contrary to other essences,' then every analogy you make between God and creation is flawed. Are you willing to let go of any comfortable but inaccurate mental image of God that you have held?

Quranic Proofs for Allah's Hearing and Sight
Razi presents Quranic evidence for divine hearing and sight and the rule for interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's scriptural argument for affirming Allah's hearing and sight and the condition under which interpretation (ta'wil) becomes necessary.

Core Idea
The Quran itself affirms Allah's hearing and sight; only a proof of impossibility justifies reinterpretation.

Razi builds a cumulative case from Quranic verses and a general principle: attributes of perfection are to be affirmed unless proven impossible. This lesson walks through his evidence and logic, showing how scripture and reason intersect in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to absorb its structure. Note the chapter heading and the flow from principle to evidence to condition.
The passage is dense; read it slowly and mark the three Quranic proofs.
2
Identify the three Quranic proofs
Razi cites three verses. Locate each and understand how each supports the affirmation of hearing and sight for Allah. The first directly states Allah hears and sees, the second implies it by contrast, and the third affirms perception while denying limitation.
3
Understand the conditional rule for interpretation
Razi introduces a key principle: affirmation is obligatory unless the opponent brings proof that the attribute is impossible for Allah. Only then does ta'wil become necessary. Note the burden of proof is on the one claiming impossibility.
This is a balanced approach—neither literalist rejection of reason nor arbitrary allegorization.
4
Reflect on the burden of proof
Consider why Razi places the burden on the opponent. What does this say about the default stance toward scriptural attributes? How does this principle apply to other divine attributes?
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about literal vs. allegorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في بيان أنه تعالى موصوف بالسمع والبصر
والدليل عليه هو أن السمع والبصر من صفات الكمال وضدهما من النقصان والقرآن وارد بإثباتهما لله تعالى قال الله تعالى : إنني معكما أسمع وأرى [طه: ٤٦] وقال إبراهيم لأبيه : يَا أَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ [مريم: ٤٢] وقال الله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَرُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَر [الأنعام: ١٠٣] وإذا كانت النصوص الكثيرة واردة بذلك وجب إثباتها لله تعالى، إلا أن يقيم الخصم دلالة على أن ثبوت هاتين الحالتين مشروط بشرط ممتنع التحقق في حق الله تعالى، فيجب المصير إلى التأويل، وتكون الدلالة إشارة إلى المعارضة، ومن ادعاها كان عليه البيان، هذا هو الذي يعول عليه في هذا الباب.
The Second Chapter: On the Demonstration that He, exalted, is described with hearing and sight. The proof for it is that hearing and sight are attributes of perfection and their opposites are [attributes] of deficiency, and the Qur'an is transmitted affirming them for Allah, exalted. Allah, exalted, said: 'Indeed, I am with you; I hear and I see' [Taha: 46]. And Ibrahim said to his father: 'O my father, why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see?' [Maryam: 42]. And Allah, exalted, said: 'Vision does not perceive Him, but He perceives vision' [al-An'am: 103]. And when many texts are transmitted regarding that, it is necessary to affirm them for Allah, exalted, unless the opponent establishes a proof that the subsistence of these two states is conditioned by a condition impossible to realize in the case of Allah, exalted. Then it becomes necessary to resort to interpretation, and the proof would be an indication of opposition. And whoever claims it must provide the clarification. This is what is relied upon in this topic.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the revelation-based proof for affirming divine hearing and sight. Razi begins with a chapter heading: 'On the Demonstration that He, exalted, is described with hearing and sight.' He states the proof: hearing and sight are attributes of perfection, while their opposites are deficiency. The Qur'an affirms them for Allah. He then quotes three verses: (1) 'Indeed, I am with you; I hear and I see' (Taha: 46) addressed to Moses and Aaron; (2) Abraham's words to his father: 'Why do you worship that which does not hear nor see?' (Maryam: 42) – implying that Allah does hear and see; (3) 'Vision does not perceive Him, but He perceives vision' (al-An'am: 103) – affirming that He sees, though He cannot be seen. Since many texts affirm these, we must affirm them unless the opponent provides proof that they require an impossible condition in Allah's case. If such proof is given, then interpretation (ta'wil) is required, but the burden of proof is on the one claiming impossibility. Razi concludes that this is the reliable approach in this matter.

Key Terms
سمع (hearing)
بصر (sight)
صفات الكمال (attributes of perfection)
النقصان (deficiency)
تأويل (interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the mere existence of a scriptural attribute requires literal interpretation without any possibility of ta'wil.
Correction: Razi allows for ta'wil if the opponent provides a rational proof that the attribute is impossible for Allah. The default is affirmation, but reason can necessitate reinterpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is required before resorting to interpretation of scriptural attributes like hearing and sight?
A) The consensus of scholars
B) The opponent must provide a proof that the attribute is impossible for Allah
C) The attribute must be mentioned in the Torah
D) The attribute must be rationally demonstrable

Key Takeaway
Razi affirms Allah's hearing and sight based on Quranic evidence and the principle that attributes of perfection are to be accepted unless proven impossible, which would then necessitate interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes Allah in a way that feels uncomfortable or anthropomorphic, do you immediately seek an interpretation, or do you first affirm it as a perfection? This lesson says affirmation is the default unless a proof of impossibility is established. Which attitude reflects your heart: trust in the divine words, or a subtle demand that Allah fit your mental categories?
?
Razi places the burden of proof on the one who claims impossibility. In your own life, when you encounter a teaching of Islam that challenges your intellect or desires, do you assume it must be reinterpreted until it aligns with your reason, or do you submit and only reinterpret when there is a decisive proof? What does your answer reveal about the state of your tawheed?
?
The verse 'Vision does not perceive Him, but He perceives vision' (al-An'am 103) affirms Allah's sight while negating that He is seen. Do you long for the vision of Allah in the hereafter? If so, how does the fact that He sees you right now — every action, every thought — shape your behavior in this moment? Does it fill you with awe or with complacency?

The Majority's Logical Proof for Divine Hearing and Sight
Razi presents the majority's logical argument for hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument used by the majority Ash'ari scholars to affirm that Allah possesses the attributes of hearing and sight, and how it relies on the impossibility of defects in Allah.

Core Idea
A living being must have hearing/sight or their opposites; since defects are impossible for Allah, He must have hearing and sight.

The majority of Ash'ari theologians formulated a syllogism to prove that Allah has the attributes of hearing and sight. This argument proceeds from Allah's life, the possibility of these attributes, and the impossibility of their opposites. In this lesson, we will examine the structure of this proof and its logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source passage (up to 'wa huwa al-maṭlūb') and its literal translation. Let the author's own words come through before analyzing.
2
Identify the components of the argument
Find the premises: Allah is living, every living can be described with hearing/sight, and the law of excluded middle. Note the conclusion.
The argument uses a logical form similar to a syllogism.
3
Understand the role of 'opposites'
Notice that the argument requires that either the attribute or its opposite must apply. The opposites (deafness/blindness) are defects, which are impossible for Allah.
This step ensures the conclusion follows necessarily.
4
Reflect on the argument's validity
Consider why the majority consider this proof sound. The logical structure is valid if the premises are accepted. Razi will later critique a premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج جمهور الأصحاب بأنه تعالى حي، وكل حي أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، وكل من صح أن يكون موصوفًا بصفة وجب أن يكون موصوفا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها ، وضد السمع والبصر هو الصمم والعمى، فثبت أنه لا بد وأن يكون الباري تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، أو بالصمم والعمى، وهذان الوصفان من باب النقائص والآفات، وهي على الله تعالى محال، ولما امتنع كونه تعالى موصوفًا بأضداد السمع والبصر وجب كونه تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر وهو المطلوب. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات يعسر تقريرها :
أما المقدمة الأولى فهي قوله : كل حي يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، فنقول : أليس كل حي يصح في المشاهد أن يكون موصوفا بالجهل والظن والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة، ثم إنه تعالى حي مع أنه لا يصح عليه شيء من ذلك؟ فعلمنا أنه لا يلزم من كونه حيا أن يصح عليه ما يصح على سائر الأحياء.
وتقرير القول فيه : أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات، وحياته تعالى مخالفة لحياة سائر الأحياء، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من صحة السمع والبصر على سائر الأحياء صحتها على ذاته وحياته.
وأيضًا بتقدير أن لا تكون ذاته المخصوصة وحياته المخصوصة منافية للسمع والبصر لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ثبوتهما مشروط بحصول هذا التأثير، ولما كان هذا الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ فهذه الاحتمالات قائمة في تحولهم : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر.
The majority of the [Ash'ari] companions argued that He, exalted, is living, and every living [being] can be described with hearing and sight. And everything of which it is correct to be described with an attribute, it is necessary that it be described with that attribute or its opposite. And the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness. So it is established that necessarily the Creator, exalted, must be described with hearing and sight, or with deafness and blindness. And these two descriptions are of the category of deficiencies and defects, and they are impossible for Allah, exalted. And since it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be described with the opposites of hearing and sight, it is necessary that He, exalted, be described with hearing and sight – and that is the desired [conclusion]. Know that this proof is based on premises whose establishment is difficult.
As for the first premise, it is his statement: 'Every living [being] can be described with hearing and sight.' We say: Does not every living [being] in the observable world be described with ignorance, conjecture, lust, aversion, pain, and pleasure? Yet He, exalted, is living while none of that is correct for Him. So we know that from His being living it does not follow that what is correct for other living beings is correct for Him.
The explanation of the statement is: His essence, exalted, is different from other essences, and His life, exalted, is different from the life of other living beings. And if that is the case, the correctness of hearing and sight for other living beings does not necessitate their correctness for His essence and life.
Also, assuming that His particular essence and His particular life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their subsistence is conditioned by the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of Allah, exalted, the conditioned [i.e., the attributes] would also be [impossible]? These are possibilities standing in their statement: He, exalted, can be described with hearing and sight.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the logical argument of the majority Ash'ari scholars for affirming divine hearing and sight, followed by Razi's critique of its first premise. The argument runs: Allah is living; every living being can be described with hearing and sight; anything that can be described with an attribute must either have it or its opposite; the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness, which are defects impossible for Allah; so Allah must have hearing and sight. Razi then notes the proof rests on difficult premises. The first premise is 'Every living being can be described with hearing and sight.' Razi objects: in the observable world, living beings can also be described with ignorance, lust, etc., which do not apply to Allah. Thus, the premise does not hold – what applies to other living beings does not necessarily apply to Allah because His essence and life are unique. Moreover, even if His essence and life are not incompatible, the attributes might require a condition that is impossible for Allah. Thus, the argument's first premise is not established.

Key Terms
جمهور الأصحاب (majority of companions)
مقدمات (premises)
ضد (opposite)
النقائص والآفات (deficiencies and defects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument proves hearing and sight are necessary attributes without requiring life.
Correction: The argument explicitly starts from Allah's life as a premise.

Checkpoint
What is the opposite of hearing and sight according to the passage?
A) Silence and darkness
B) Deafness and blindness
C) Ignorance and insensitivity
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The majority proof for divine hearing and sight is a logical syllogism based on the impossibility of defects in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's hearing and sight are not optional but a logical necessity of His perfection, how would that change the way you speak about Him in your daily life? What words would you stop using?
?
When you sin in secret, do you act as if Allah's sight is a mere belief rather than an inescapable reality? What would it take for you to feel the weight of His seeing you in that moment?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of defects in Allah. Where in your own life have you made excuses for spiritual 'defects' (like negligence or disobedience) as if they were acceptable? How can you treat them as truly impossible for a believer to accept?

Razi's Critique of the First Premise
Razi critiques the first premise using the uniqueness of Allah and potential conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the first premise of the majority's argument, emphasizing the uniqueness of Allah's essence and life and the possibility of conditions.

Core Idea
What applies to other living beings does not automatically apply to Allah due to His unique essence and life.

Razi begins his critique by questioning the first premise: 'Every living being can be described with hearing and sight.' He argues that observable living beings also have defects like ignorance and lust, which are impossible for Allah. Therefore, the premise does not transfer to Allah. He deepens this by noting that Allah's essence and life are fundamentally different from other beings, and even if not incompatible, the attributes might require a condition impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the second part of the source passage (from 'amma al-muqaddima al-ula') and its literal translation. Pay attention to Razi's objections.
2
Identify the two main objections
First, Razi points out that other living beings have defects that cannot apply to Allah. Second, he argues that Allah's essence and life are unique, so attributes do not transfer directly.
He also offers a conditional argument.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Even if the essence is not incompatible, the attributes might depend on a condition that is impossible for Allah. This further undermines the premise.
Razi concludes that the premise is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج جمهور الأصحاب بأنه تعالى حي، وكل حي أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، وكل من صح أن يكون موصوفًا بصفة وجب أن يكون موصوفا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها ، وضد السمع والبصر هو الصمم والعمى، فثبت أنه لا بد وأن يكون الباري تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، أو بالصمم والعمى، وهذان الوصفان من باب النقائص والآفات، وهي على الله تعالى محال، ولما امتنع كونه تعالى موصوفًا بأضداد السمع والبصر وجب كونه تعالى موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر وهو المطلوب. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات يعسر تقريرها :
أما المقدمة الأولى فهي قوله : كل حي يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر، فنقول : أليس كل حي يصح في المشاهد أن يكون موصوفا بالجهل والظن والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة، ثم إنه تعالى حي مع أنه لا يصح عليه شيء من ذلك؟ فعلمنا أنه لا يلزم من كونه حيا أن يصح عليه ما يصح على سائر الأحياء.

The majority of the [Ash'ari] companions argued that He, exalted, is living, and every living [being] can be described with hearing and sight. And everything of which it is correct to be described with an attribute, it is necessary that it be described with that attribute or its opposite. And the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness. So it is established that necessarily the Creator, exalted, must be described with hearing and sight, or with deafness and blindness. And these two descriptions are of the category of deficiencies and defects, and they are impossible for Allah, exalted. And since it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be described with the opposites of hearing and sight, it is necessary that He, exalted, be described with hearing and sight – and that is the desired [conclusion]. Know that this proof is based on premises whose establishment is difficult.
As for the first premise, it is his statement: 'Every living [being] can be described with hearing and sight.' We say: Does not every living [being] in the observable world be described with ignorance, conjecture, lust, aversion, pain, and pleasure? Yet He, exalted, is living while none of that is correct for Him. So we know that from His being living it does not follow that what is correct for other living beings is correct for Him.
The explanation of the statement is: His essence, exalted, is different from other essences, and His life, exalted, is different from the life of other living beings. And if that is the case, the correctness of hearing and sight for other living beings does not necessitate their correctness for His essence and life.
Also, assuming that His particular essence and His particular life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their subsistence is conditioned by the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of Allah, exalted, the conditioned [i.e., the attributes] would also be [impossible]? These are possibilities standing in their statement: He, exalted, can be described with hearing and sight.

This passage presents the logical argument of the majority Ash'ari scholars for affirming divine hearing and sight, followed by Razi's critique of its first premise. The argument runs: Allah is living; every living being can be described with hearing and sight; anything that can be described with an attribute must either have it or its opposite; the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness, which are defects impossible for Allah; so Allah must have hearing and sight. Moreover, even if His essence and life are not incompatible, the attributes might require a condition that is impossible for Allah. Thus, the argument's first premise is not established.

المقدمة الأولى (first premise)
ذاته (His essence)
حياته (His life)
مخالفة (different)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Razi is denying that Allah can have hearing and sight.
Correction: Razi is only critiquing the logical proof, not denying the attributes themselves.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the first premise not apply to Allah?
A) Because Allah is not living
B) Because other living beings have defects that cannot apply to Allah
C) Because hearing and sight are not perfections
D) Because the premise contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
Razi critiques the first premise by arguing that Allah's unique essence and life prevent automatic transfer of attributes from other living beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'Hearing' and 'Seeing,' do you ever imagine those attributes in a human-like way? How does Razi's reminder that Allah's essence and life are completely different challenge you to purify your mental image of Allah?
?
We often compare Allah to things we know to make sense of faith, but Razi shows this can lead to error. Is there an area in your life where you have unconsciously measured Allah's actions by human standards (e.g., expecting Him to act as a human would)? What would it take for you to surrender that comparison and truly believe that Allah is unlike anything?
?
Razi mentions that attributes like ignorance and lust are found in other living beings but impossible for Allah. Take a moment: Are there any negative traits you associate with yourself that you would never ascribe to Allah? Now, are there any positive traits you are tempted to limit by your own experience? Commit to renewing your tawhid by affirming that Allah's perfection is beyond any comparison.

Razi's Critique of the Second Premise: Opposite as Privation vs. Positive
Razi analyzes the second premise concerning necessity of an attribute or its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical dissection of the second premise, distinguishing between absence and positive contraries, and why the burden of proof lies on those claiming opposites are positive realities.

Core Idea
An 'opposite' may be a mere absence, not a positive reality—check your assumptions.

Razi now tackles the second premise of the majority's argument: that everything capable of an attribute must have either that attribute or its opposite. He shows that this premise is either trivial or unproven. The key is what you mean by 'opposite'—mere absence or a positive contrary? Razi argues that the opponents must prove that blindness and deafness are positive realities, not just privations. This lesson walks through his careful logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two interpretations
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that Razi presents two possible meanings for 'opposite of the attribute': absence of the attribute, or a positive incompatible quality.
Look for the phrases 'إذا أردتم...' and 'وإن أردتم...'
2
Understand Razi's refutation of the first interpretation
If 'opposite' means absence, the premise becomes trivial (thing either has attribute or not). But the opponent needs to show that not having the attribute is impossible—which is exactly what is disputed. So it cannot be a premise.
3
Examine the second interpretation and the philosophers' position
If 'opposite' means a positive contrary (like black vs. white), Razi asks why we should assume hearing and sight are opposites in this sense. Philosophers say the opposition is privation vs. possession (absence of a quality in something that normally has it), not contraries. This shifts the burden of proof.
Privation (ʿadam al-milka) is not a positive entity.
4
Reflect on the ultimate burden of proof
Even if we cannot determine which interpretation is correct in reality, the opponent must provide evidence. Razi shows that the second premise is not a solid foundation for the argument.
This is a classic example of logical analysis in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الثانية وهي قولهم : كل ما يصح اتصافه بصفة فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موصوفًا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها.
فنقول : إن أردتم بضد الصفة عدم الصفة كان المعنى أن كل ما اتصافه صح بصفة فإما أن يكون موصوفا بها وإما أن لا يكون وهذا حق، ولكن لم قلتم إن عدم اتصافه بتلك الصفة محال؟ فإن هذا عين المتنازع فيه ولا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المتنازع، وإن أردتم بضد الصفة معنى وجوديًا منافيًا لتلك الصفة على وزان المنافيات الحاصلة من السواد والبياض فلم قلتم : إن السمع والبصر ضدان بهذا المعنى ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر عما شأنه أن من يبصر، والصمم عبارة عن عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يسمع ؟ فأنتم في هذا المقام محتاجون إلى إثبات أن الصمم والعمى معنيان موجودان مضادان للسمع والبصر. والفلاسفة ينازعون فيه أشد المنازعة، فإنهم يقولون : تقابل البصر والعمى وتقابل السمع والصمم تقابل العدم والملكة لا تقابل الضدين وقول من قال : ليس جعل العمى عدما للبصر أولى من العكس ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنه إن أريد هذه بعدم الأولوية عدمها في أذهاننا وعقولنا فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا لا ينتج إلا أن نتوقف فيه، ولا نقطع على أحد الجانبين، وإن أريد بعدم هذه الأولوية عدمها في نفس الأمر وفي الحقيقة فهذا ممنوع، ولعل هذه الأولوية حاصلة في نفس الأمر، وإن كنا لا نعرف كيفية تلك الأولوية.
As for the second premise – and it is their statement: 'Everything of which it is correct to be described with an attribute, it is necessary that it be described with that attribute or its opposite.' We say: If you mean by 'opposite of the attribute' the absence of the attribute, then the meaning is that everything of which description with an attribute is correct is either described with it or not – and this is true. But why do you say that the absence of description with that attribute is impossible? For this is precisely the point of disagreement, and it cannot be made a premise in proving the disputed matter. And if you mean by 'opposite of the attribute' a positive meaning incompatible with that attribute, analogous to the incompatibilities occurring from blackness and whiteness, then why do you say that hearing and sight are opposites in this sense? And why is it not possible to say that blindness is the absence of sight from what is supposed to see, and deafness is the absence of hearing from what is supposed to hear? For you are in need, at this point, of proving that deafness and blindness are two existing meanings that are opposites of hearing and sight. The philosophers dispute this most strenuously, for they say that the opposition of sight and blindness and the opposition of hearing and deafness is the opposition of privation and possession, not the opposition of contraries. And the statement of one who says 'making blindness an absence of sight is not more appropriate than the opposite' is clearly invalid, because if what is intended by 'lack of appropriateness' is its absence in our minds and intellects, then this is conceded, but it only results in suspension [of judgment], and we do not decide on either side. And if what is intended by 'lack of appropriateness' is its absence in the matter itself and in reality, then this is denied; perhaps this appropriateness is present in the matter itself, even if we do not know the modality of that appropriateness.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage critiques the second premise of the majority's argument: that anything capable of an attribute must necessarily be described either with that attribute or its opposite. Razi distinguishes two interpretations. If 'opposite' means mere absence (i.e., not having the attribute), then the statement is true but trivial – it simply says something either has the attribute or not. But the opponent needs to show that not having the attribute is impossible, which is precisely what is being contested. If 'opposite' means a positive contrary (like black vs. white), then one must prove that deafness and blindness are positive realities, not mere privations. Razi notes that philosophers argue that the opposition here is privation vs. possession (like sight and blindness), not contraries. If one claims it is arbitrary to consider blindness as absence, that is false – absence of sight is logically prior for beings that normally see. Even if we cannot determine the appropriateness in reality, the burden of proof is on the original arguer.

Key Terms
المقدمة الثانية (second premise)
ضد (opposite)
عدم (absence/non-existence)
معنى وجودي (positive meaning)
تقابل العدم والملكة (privation and possession)
الفلاسفة (philosophers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is denying that Allah has hearing and sight or that these attributes are real.
Correction: Razi is not denying the attributes; he is showing that the logical argument used to prove them is flawed. The premise is either trivial or unproven.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do philosophers claim about the opposition of sight and blindness?
A) They are contraries like black and white
B) They are relative terms
C) They are the opposition of privation and possession
D) They are identical in meaning

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the second premise is either trivial or unproven, shifting the burden of proof to those who claim that opposites are positive realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself insisting that something must be either X or its opposite, do you ever pause to check whether the 'opposite' is actually a positive reality or just the absence of X? What might this lesson reveal about your own intellectual habits in matters of faith? Will you commit to examining your assumptions before building arguments about Allah's attributes?
?
Razi shifts the burden of proof to those who claim deafness and blindness are positive opposites. In your own life, do you sometimes assert positions without realizing the burden of proof is on you? How ready are you to admit that an argument you rely on may be unsupported? What would it look like for you to take intellectual responsibility in your beliefs about Allah?

Refuting the Third Premise: Counterexamples
Razi refutes the third premise and provides counterexamples to show it is not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the third premise in the argument for divine attributes, specifically his use of counterexamples to show that a substrate capable of two opposites need not possess either.

Core Idea
Capable of opposites does not guarantee possession of one.

Razi continues his analysis of the logical premises used to argue that Allah must have certain attributes. The third premise states that if two attributes are contrary opposites, any substrate capable of both must possess one. But is that logically necessary? Razi challenges this and provides concrete counterexamples to show the premise is false. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument flow before you interpret.
Notice that Razi directly addresses the opponent in a questioning tone.
2
Identify the third premise
Razi restates the third premise: if blindness and deafness are contraries of hearing and sight, then every essence capable of both must be described with one. Find this statement and note his challenge.
The premise is stated as a conditional: 'if... then...'
3
Examine the counterexamples
Razi gives two examples: air devoid of all colors and tastes, and a person indifferent to sinful actions. Ask yourself how these show the premise is false.
Air is capable of colors and tastes but has neither; the person is capable of desire or aversion but has neither.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Reflecting on how this refutation weakens the logical proof for divine attributes. Does it mean Allah lacks attributes? No, only that this specific argument fails.
Razi is not denying divine attributes; he is critiquing the logical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن بتقدير أن يكون العمى والصمم متقابلين تقابل التضاد لم قلتم : إن كل ذات تكون قابلة للضدين بهذا التفسير، فإنه لا بد أن تكون موصوفة بأحدهما ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه قد تكون خالية عنهما ؟ فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدلالة على إثبات هذه المقدمة.
ثم إنا ننقض هذه المقدمة : بأن الهواء خال من جميع الألوان ومن جميع الطعوم، والواحد قد لا يكون مريدا لأفعال أهل الفسوق ولا كارها لها، فبطلت هذه المقدمة.
As for the third premise – and it is that assuming blindness and deafness are opposed by opposition of contraries, why do you say that every essence capable of the two opposites in this interpretation must necessarily be described with one of them? And why is it not possible to say that it may be devoid of both? For they are required to establish proof for this premise. Then we refute this premise by [the fact that] air is devoid of all colors and all tastes, and a person may not be desiring of the actions of sinful people nor averse to them. So this premise is invalidated.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses the third premise: even if deafness and blindness are positive contraries of hearing and sight, why must every substrate capable of both opposites necessarily possess one? Razi asks: why can it not be devoid of both? The opponent must prove this. Then Razi provides counterexamples: air has no color and no taste (it is capable of both but has neither); similarly, a person may be indifferent to sinful actions – not desiring nor averse. Thus, the premise that a substrate must have one of two contrary attributes is false. This refutes the logical argument.

Key Terms
المقدمة الثالثة (third premise)
تقابل التضاد (opposition of contraries)
قابلة للضدين (capable of the two opposites)
نقض (refutation)
مثال (example)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two attributes are true opposites, any entity that can have both must have one.
Correction: Razi shows this is not always true; e.g., air has no color nor taste, yet it is capable of both.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does Razi use to refute the third premise?
A) Water is hot or cold
B) Air is devoid of colors and tastes
C) Fire is both hot and dry
D) Man is either awake or asleep

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the third premise by showing that being capable of two opposites does not necessitate possessing one; counterexamples like air lacking color and taste prove the premise false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi challenges a seemingly reasonable premise with counterexamples. What core belief about Allah do you hold that you have never critically examined? Will you dare to question it, trusting that truth can withstand scrutiny?
?
The passage shows that capability does not necessitate actuality. In your life, are you feeling pressured to choose between two extremes (e.g., in a conflict or decision)? How might Razi's insight free you to remain neutral or seek a third option?
?
Razi demands proof for a premise that his opponents took for granted. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on assumptions rather than evidence? Commit today to seeking deeper understanding of one such belief, even if it unsettles you.

Refuting the Conditional Necessity of Hearing and Sight
Razi refutes the first premise by introducing a conditional necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi questions the necessity of affirming hearing and sight for God even if His essence is not incompatible with them.

Core Idea
Even if God can have an attribute, its actual possession may require an impossible condition.

Razi begins by conceding that God's essence and life might not be incompatible with hearing and sight. However, he then challenges the assumption that this automatically makes them necessary. He introduces the idea that these attributes might be conditional on an effect that cannot obtain in God, so they remain possible but not actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the initial concession and the conditional argument.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Notice that Razi uses a conditional 'if... then' structure: if the condition (shart) is impossible, then the conditioned (mashrut) is also impossible.
The condition is the occurrence of an effect (ta'thir).
3
Understand the implication
This shows that the opponent cannot simply assert that because God's essence does not preclude hearing, He must have it; there may be a missing condition.
4
Reflect on the burden of proof
Razi leaves the opponent with possibilities that undermine their claim. The opponent must prove that no such condition exists.
This is a common dialectical strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة عشرة

وأيضًا بتقدير أن لا تكون ذاته المخصوصة وحياته المخصوصة منافية للسمع والبصر لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ثبوتهما مشروط بحصول هذا التأثير، ولما كان هذا الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ فهذه الاحتمالات قائمة في تحولهم : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر.

أما المقدمة الثانية وهي قولهم : كل ما يصح اتصافه بصفة فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موصوفًا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها.

فنقول : إن أردتم بضد الصفة عدم الصفة كان المعنى أن كل ما اتصافه صح بصفة فإما أن يكون موصوفا بها وإما أن لا يكون وهذا حق، ولكن لم قلتم إن عدم اتصافه بتلك الصفة محال؟ فإن هذا عين المتنازع فيه ولا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المتنازع، وإن أردتم بضد الصفة معنى وجوديًا منافيًا لتلك الصفة على وزان المنافيات الحاصلة من السواد والبياض فلم قلتم : إن السمع والبصر ضدان بهذا المعنى ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر عما شأنه أن من يبصر، والصمم عبارة عن عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يسمع ؟ فأنتم في هذا المقام محتاجون إلى إثبات أن الصمم والعمى معنيان موجودان مضادان للسمع والبصر. والفلاسفة ينازعون فيه أشد المنازعة، فإنهم يقولون : تقابل البصر والعمى وتقابل السمع والصمم تقابل العدم والملكة لا تقابل الضدين وقول من قال : ليس جعل العمى عدما للبصر أولى من العكس ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنه إن أريد هذه بعدم الأولوية عدمها في أذهاننا وعقولنا فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا لا ينتج إلا أن نتوقف فيه، ولا نقطع على أحد الجانبين، وإن أريد بعدم هذه الأولوية عدمها في نفس الأمر وفي الحقيقة فهذا ممنوع، ولعل هذه الأولوية حاصلة في نفس الأمر، وإن كنا لا نعرف كيفية تلك الأولوية. أما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن بتقدير أن يكون العمى والصمم متقابلين تقابل التضاد لم قلتم : إن كل ذات تكون قابلة للضدين بهذا التفسير، فإنه لا بد أن تكون موصوفة بأحدهما ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه قد تكون خالية عنهما ؟ فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدلالة على إثبات هذه المقدمة.

ثم إنا ننقض هذه المقدمة : بأن الهواء خال من جميع الألوان ومن جميع الطعوم، والواحد قد لا يكون مريدا لأفعال أهل الفسوق ولا كارها لها، فبطلت هذه المقدمة.

أما المقدمة الرابعة - وهي قوله : إنه لا يمكن اتصافه تعالى بالعمى والصمم؛ لأن ذلك من صفات النقص، وصفات النقص على الله تعالى محال - أنهم عولوا في تنزيه الله تعالى عن النقائص على الإجماع، ثم إنهم يثبتون كون الإجماع حجة بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث، فصارت هذه الدلالة بالآخرة سمعية، ثم إنا نرى أن الظواهر الدالة على كونه سميعا بصيرًا أقوى من الظواهر الدالة على أن الإجماع حجة وأكثر، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فبأن نتمسك في إثبات كونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بهذه الظواهر القوية، ونسقط عن أنفسنا التزام تقرير هذه المقدمات الخفية المظلمة، كان أولى فهذا ما نقوله في هذا الباب.
The sixteenth issue: And also, assuming that His specific essence and His specific life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their affirmation is conditional upon the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of God Almighty, then the conditional is also likewise? So these possibilities stand in their claim that God Almighty can be described with hearing and sight. As for the second premise, which is their statement: 'Everything whose description with an attribute is valid must necessarily be described with that attribute or its opposite.' We say: If you mean by the opposite of the attribute the absence of the attribute, then the meaning is that everything whose description with an attribute is valid is either described with it or not—and this is true. But why do you say that its not being described with that attribute is impossible? For this is the very point of dispute, and it cannot be made a premise in proving the disputed point. And if you mean by the opposite of the attribute an existent meaning contrary to that attribute, on the model of the contrarieties occurring from black and white, then why do you say that hearing and sight are opposites in this sense? And why is it not permissible to say that blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to see, and deafness is the absence of hearing from that which is supposed to hear? You, in this position, are in need of proving that deafness and blindness are two existent meanings opposite to hearing and sight. The philosophers dispute this most vehemently, for they say: The opposition between sight and blindness and between hearing and deafness is the opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa-l-milka), not the opposition of contraries. And the statement of whoever says: 'Making blindness an absence of sight is not more prior than the reverse' is manifestly false; because if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in our minds and intellects, then this is admitted. However, this yields only that we suspend judgment, and we do not decide on either side. And if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in the matter itself and in reality, then this is denied. Perhaps this priority is realized in the matter itself, even if we do not know the manner of that priority. As for the third premise: That assuming blindness and deafness are opposites in the sense of contrariety, why do you say that every essence capable of the two opposites in this interpretation must necessarily be described with one of them? And why is it not permissible to say that it may be devoid of both? They are required to provide evidence for proving this premise. Then we refute this premise: That air is devoid of all colors and all tastes, and one may not be willing for the actions of the people of immorality nor averse to them. Thus this premise is invalid. As for the fourth premise—which is his statement: 'It is impossible for God Almighty to be described with blindness and deafness, because that is from the attributes of deficiency, and attributes of deficiency are impossible for God Almighty'—they relied in declaring God Almighty free of deficiencies on consensus (ijmāʿ). Then they prove that consensus is a proof by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths. So this proof becomes ultimately scriptural (samʿiyya). Then we see that the apparent meanings indicating that He is hearing and seeing are stronger and more numerous than the apparent meanings indicating that consensus is a proof. And when the matter is so, then for us to adhere in proving that God Almighty is hearing and seeing to these strong apparent meanings, and to drop from ourselves the obligation of articulating these obscure, dark premises, is more appropriate. This is what we say in this chapter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple premises used by those who argue that God must be described with hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). Razi first allows that God's essence and life might not be incompatible with these attributes, but then questions the necessity of their affirmation. He examines the second premise—that whatever can be described with an attribute must have it or its opposite—by distinguishing two interpretations of 'opposite.' If it means mere absence, then the premise is trivial and does not prove the impossibility of lacking the attribute, which is the very point in dispute. If it means an existent contrary (like black vs. white), then the opponent must prove that deafness and blindness are existent opposites, not merely privations. Razi appeals to the philosophers who hold that the opposition is privation and possession (ʿadam wa-milka), not contrariety. The third premise—that whatever can receive two opposites must have one—is refuted by counterexamples: air has no color or taste, and a person may neither will nor detest certain actions. The fourth premise—that God cannot have deficiency—is based on consensus, which itself relies on scriptural evidence. Razi argues that the scriptural indications for God's hearing and sight are stronger and more numerous, so it is better to rely on them directly rather than on the weak premises presented by the opponents.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's essence is not incompatible with hearing and sight, then He must possess them.
Correction: Possession may be conditional on a further condition that is impossible for God, so incompatibility is not the only barrier.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the condition (shart) for the affirmation of hearing and sight?
A) The occurrence of an effect (ta'thir)
B) The absence of incompatibility
C) The existence of the opposite
D) The consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Even if God's essence allows an attribute, its actuality may depend on a condition that is impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is not clearly impossible for God, it must be true? How does Razi's conditional argument challenge you to examine the conditions you place on God's actions? This week, when you make dua, will you reflect on whether you are assuming God can do something based only on lack of contradiction, or are you truly submitting to His will and wisdom?
?
Razi shows that even if God's essence allows an attribute, its actuality may depend on a condition that is impossible for Him. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that because God is All-Powerful, He must grant your wishes? How does this lesson correct that assumption? What condition might God have placed that you are ignoring?
?
The opponent's argument seemed logical, but Razi exposes a hidden assumption. What hidden assumptions do you hold about God that you have never examined? Will you challenge them today, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Divine Attributes

The Second Premise: Attribute and Its Opposite
Razi refutes the second premise by distinguishing two meanings of 'opposite'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's distinction between two meanings of 'opposite' and why the second premise fails either way.

Core Idea
The second premise is either trivial or unproven, depending on whether 'opposite' means absence or an existent contrary.

Razi now tackles the second premise, which claims that if something can have an attribute, it must have either that attribute or its opposite. He breaks down this premise by examining the meaning of 'opposite' and shows that it does not advance the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise and Razi's response
Read the passage carefully. Notice that Razi offers two interpretations of 'opposite'.
2
Analyze the first interpretation
If 'opposite' means absence, then the premise is trivially true (either it has the attribute or not). But this does not prove that lacking the attribute is impossible, which is the point in dispute.
Razi says: 'why do you say that its not being described with that attribute is impossible?'
3
Analyze the second interpretation
If 'opposite' means an existent contrary, then the opponent must prove that blindness and deafness are genuine existent opposites, not mere privations.
Razi appeals to the philosophers who argue that the opposition is privation and possession, not contrariety.
4
Understand the philosophers' position
The philosophers distinguish between opposites like black-white (contraries) and pairs like sight-blindness (privation/possession). In the latter, blindness is not an existent quality but the absence of sight.
Razi uses this to undermine the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة عشرة

وأيضًا بتقدير أن لا تكون ذاته المخصوصة وحياته المخصوصة منافية للسمع والبصر لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ثبوتهما مشروط بحصول هذا التأثير، ولما كان هذا الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ فهذه الاحتمالات قائمة في تحولهم : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر.

أما المقدمة الثانية وهي قولهم : كل ما يصح اتصافه بصفة فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موصوفًا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها.

فنقول : إن أردتم بضد الصفة عدم الصفة كان المعنى أن كل ما اتصافه صح بصفة فإما أن يكون موصوفا بها وإما أن لا يكون وهذا حق، ولكن لم قلتم إن عدم اتصافه بتلك الصفة محال؟ فإن هذا عين المتنازع فيه ولا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المتنازع، وإن أردتم بضد الصفة معنى وجوديًا منافيًا لتلك الصفة على وزان المنافيات الحاصلة من السواد والبياض فلم قلتم : إن السمع والبصر ضدان بهذا المعنى ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر عما شأنه أن من يبصر، والصمم عبارة عن عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يسمع ؟ فأنتم في هذا المقام محتاجون إلى إثبات أن الصمم والعمى معنيان موجودان مضادان للسمع والبصر. والفلاسفة ينازعون فيه أشد المنازعة، فإنهم يقولون : تقابل البصر والعمى وتقابل السمع والصمم تقابل العدم والملكة لا تقابل الضدين وقول من قال : ليس جعل العمى عدما للبصر أولى من العكس ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنه إن أريد هذه بعدم الأولوية عدمها في أذهاننا وعقولنا فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا لا ينتج إلا أن نتوقف فيه، ولا نقطع على أحد الجانبين، وإن أريد بعدم هذه الأولوية عدمها في نفس الأمر وفي الحقيقة فهذا ممنوع، ولعل هذه الأولوية حاصلة في نفس الأمر، وإن كنا لا نعرف كيفية تلك الأولوية. أما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن بتقدير أن يكون العمى والصمم متقابلين تقابل التضاد لم قلتم : إن كل ذات تكون قابلة للضدين بهذا التفسير، فإنه لا بد أن تكون موصوفة بأحدهما ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه قد تكون خالية عنهما ؟ فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدلالة على إثبات هذه المقدمة.

ثم إنا ننقض هذه المقدمة : بأن الهواء خال من جميع الألوان ومن جميع الطعوم، والواحد قد لا يكون مريدا لأفعال أهل الفسوق ولا كارها لها، فبطلت هذه المقدمة.

أما المقدمة الرابعة - وهي قوله : إنه لا يمكن اتصافه تعالى بالعمى والصمم؛ لأن ذلك من صفات النقص، وصفات النقص على الله تعالى محال - أنهم عولوا في تنزيه الله تعالى عن النقائص على الإجماع، ثم إنهم يثبتون كون الإجماع حجة بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث، فصارت هذه الدلالة بالآخرة سمعية، ثم إنا نرى أن الظواهر الدالة على كونه سميعا بصيرًا أقوى من الظواهر الدالة على أن الإجماع حجة وأكثر، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فبأن نتمسك في إثبات كونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بهذه الظواهر القوية، ونسقط عن أنفسنا التزام تقرير هذه المقدمات الخفية المظلمة، كان أولى فهذا ما نقوله في هذا الباب.
The sixteenth issue: And also, assuming that His specific essence and His specific life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their affirmation is conditional upon the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of God Almighty, then the conditional is also likewise? So these possibilities stand in their claim that God Almighty can be described with hearing and sight. As for the second premise, which is their statement: 'Everything whose description with an attribute is valid must necessarily be described with that attribute or its opposite.' We say: If you mean by the opposite of the attribute the absence of the attribute, then the meaning is that everything whose description with an attribute is valid is either described with it or not—and this is true. But why do you say that its not being described with that attribute is impossible? For this is the very point of dispute, and it cannot be made a premise in proving the disputed point. And if you mean by the opposite of the attribute an existent meaning contrary to that attribute, on the model of the contrarieties occurring from black and white, then why do you say that hearing and sight are opposites in this sense? And why is it not permissible to say that blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to see, and deafness is the absence of hearing from that which is supposed to hear? You, in this position, are in need of proving that deafness and blindness are two existent meanings opposite to hearing and sight. The philosophers dispute this most vehemently, for they say: The opposition between sight and blindness and between hearing and deafness is the opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa-l-milka), not the opposition of contraries. And the statement of whoever says: 'Making blindness an absence of sight is not more prior than the reverse' is manifestly false; because if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in our minds and intellects, then this is admitted. However, this yields only that we suspend judgment, and we do not decide on either side. And if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in the matter itself and in reality, then this is denied. Perhaps this priority is realized in the matter itself, even if we do not know the manner of that priority. As for the third premise: That assuming blindness and deafness are opposites in the sense of contrariety, why do you say that every essence capable of the two opposites in this interpretation must necessarily be described with one of them? And why is it not permissible to say that it may be devoid of both? They are required to provide evidence for proving this premise. Then we refute this premise: That air is devoid of all colors and all tastes, and one may not be willing for the actions of the people of immorality nor averse to them. Thus this premise is invalid. As for the fourth premise—which is his statement: 'It is impossible for God Almighty to be described with blindness and deafness, because that is from the attributes of deficiency, and attributes of deficiency are impossible for God Almighty'—they relied in declaring God Almighty free of deficiencies on consensus (ijmāʿ). Then they prove that consensus is a proof by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths. So this proof becomes ultimately scriptural (samʿiyya). Then we see that the apparent meanings indicating that He is hearing and seeing are stronger and more numerous than the apparent meanings indicating that consensus is a proof. And when the matter is so, then for us to adhere in proving that God Almighty is hearing and seeing to these strong apparent meanings, and to drop from ourselves the obligation of articulating these obscure, dark premises, is more appropriate. This is what we say in this chapter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple premises used by those who argue that God must be described with hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). Razi first allows that God's essence and life might not be incompatible with these attributes, but then questions the necessity of their affirmation. He examines the second premise—that whatever can be described with an attribute must have it or its opposite—by distinguishing two interpretations of 'opposite.' If it means mere absence, then the premise is trivial and does not prove the impossibility of lacking the attribute, which is the very point in dispute. If it means an existent contrary (like black vs. white), then the opponent must prove that deafness and blindness are existent opposites, not merely privations. Razi appeals to the philosophers who hold that the opposition is privation and possession (ʿadam wa-milka), not contrariety. The third premise—that whatever can receive two opposites must have one—is refuted by counterexamples: air has no color or taste, and a person may neither will nor detest certain actions. The fourth premise—that God cannot have deficiency—is based on consensus, which itself relies on scriptural evidence. Razi argues that the scriptural indications for God's hearing and sight are stronger and more numerous, so it is better to rely on them directly rather than on the weak premises presented by the opponents.

Key Terms
ضد
عدم
تقابل العدم والملكة
عمى
صمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'opposite' is univocal; blindness and deafness are like black and white.
Correction: Opposition can be privation/possession, where the 'opposite' is not an existent attribute but the absence of it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do the philosophers say about the opposition between sight and blindness?
A) They are genuine contraries like black and white.
B) They are related as privation and possession.
C) They are not opposites at all.
D) Blindness is an existent attribute opposite to sight.

Key Takeaway
The second premise fails because 'opposite' can mean mere absence, which is trivial, or an existent contrary, which is unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use terms like 'opposite' to argue about God, are you certain that your definition is the only valid one? How would you react if someone showed that your premise is either trivial or unproven?
?
Razi points out that priority may be real even if we don't know its manner. Are you comfortable admitting that some truths about God are beyond your understanding, or do you insist on full clarity before accepting them?
?
The philosophers' view that blindness is a privation challenges a common assumption. Is there an assumption you hold about God's attributes that you have never critically examined? What would it cost you to examine it honestly?

The Third Premise: Necessity of One Opposite
Razi refutes the third premise with counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the premise that a substrate capable of opposites must always be characterized by one of them, using counterexamples.

Core Idea
A thing can be devoid of both opposites, as air is colorless and tasteless.

The third premise asserts that if something can receive two opposites, it must actually have one of them. Razi refutes this by providing counterexamples from physical and moral domains.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise and its refutation
Read the passage carefully. Notice Razi's initial question and then the counterexamples.
2
Identify the first counterexample
Air is devoid of all colors and all tastes. Yet air is capable of receiving colors and tastes (e.g., it can be colored by light or flavored by particles).
This shows that capability does not necessitate actual possession of one opposite.
3
Identify the second counterexample
A person may neither will (irāda) nor detest (karāha) certain actions. The person is capable of both but may be indifferent.
This is from the moral domain.
4
Understand the conclusion
These examples invalidate the premise, as they show cases where a substrate capable of opposites has neither.
The opponent must prove that the premise holds universally, which is refuted.

Source Text

المسألة السادسة عشرة

وأيضًا بتقدير أن لا تكون ذاته المخصوصة وحياته المخصوصة منافية للسمع والبصر لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ثبوتهما مشروط بحصول هذا التأثير، ولما كان هذا الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ فهذه الاحتمالات قائمة في تحولهم : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر.

أما المقدمة الثانية وهي قولهم : كل ما يصح اتصافه بصفة فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موصوفًا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها.

فنقول : إن أردتم بضد الصفة عدم الصفة كان المعنى أن كل ما اتصافه صح بصفة فإما أن يكون موصوفا بها وإما أن لا يكون وهذا حق، ولكن لم قلتم إن عدم اتصافه بتلك الصفة محال؟ فإن هذا عين المتنازع فيه ولا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المتنازع، وإن أردتم بضد الصفة معنى وجوديًا منافيًا لتلك الصفة على وزان المنافيات الحاصلة من السواد والبياض فلم قلتم : إن السمع والبصر ضدان بهذا المعنى ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر عما شأنه أن من يبصر، والصمم عبارة عن عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يسمع ؟ فأنتم في هذا المقام محتاجون إلى إثبات أن الصمم والعمى معنيان موجودان مضادان للسمع والبصر. والفلاسفة ينازعون فيه أشد المنازعة، فإنهم يقولون : تقابل البصر والعمى وتقابل السمع والصمم تقابل العدم والملكة لا تقابل الضدين وقول من قال : ليس جعل العمى عدما للبصر أولى من العكس ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنه إن أريد هذه بعدم الأولوية عدمها في أذهاننا وعقولنا فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا لا ينتج إلا أن نتوقف فيه، ولا نقطع على أحد الجانبين، وإن أريد بعدم هذه الأولوية عدمها في نفس الأمر وفي الحقيقة فهذا ممنوع، ولعل هذه الأولوية حاصلة في نفس الأمر، وإن كنا لا نعرف كيفية تلك الأولوية. أما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن بتقدير أن يكون العمى والصمم متقابلين تقابل التضاد لم قلتم : إن كل ذات تكون قابلة للضدين بهذا التفسير، فإنه لا بد أن تكون موصوفة بأحدهما ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه قد تكون خالية عنهما ؟ فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدلالة على إثبات هذه المقدمة.

ثم إنا ننقض هذه المقدمة : بأن الهواء خال من جميع الألوان ومن جميع الطعوم، والواحد قد لا يكون مريدا لأفعال أهل الفسوق ولا كارها لها، فبطلت هذه المقدمة.

أما المقدمة الرابعة - وهي قوله : إنه لا يمكن اتصافه تعالى بالعمى والصمم؛ لأن ذلك من صفات النقص، وصفات النقص على الله تعالى محال - أنهم عولوا في تنزيه الله تعالى عن النقائص على الإجماع، ثم إنهم يثبتون كون الإجماع حجة بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث، فصارت هذه الدلالة بالآخرة سمعية، ثم إنا نرى أن الظواهر الدالة على كونه سميعا بصيرًا أقوى من الظواهر الدالة على أن الإجماع حجة وأكثر، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فبأن نتمسك في إثبات كونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بهذه الظواهر القوية، ونسقط عن أنفسنا التزام تقرير هذه المقدمات الخفية المظلمة، كان أولى فهذا ما نقوله في هذا الباب.
The sixteenth issue: And also, assuming that His specific essence and His specific life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their affirmation is conditional upon the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of God Almighty, then the conditional is also likewise? So these possibilities stand in their claim that God Almighty can be described with hearing and sight. As for the second premise, which is their statement: 'Everything whose description with an attribute is valid must necessarily be described with that attribute or its opposite.' We say: If you mean by the opposite of the attribute the absence of the attribute, then the meaning is that everything whose description with an attribute is valid is either described with it or not—and this is true. But why do you say that its not being described with that attribute is impossible? For this is the very point of dispute, and it cannot be made a premise in proving the disputed point. And if you mean by the opposite of the attribute an existent meaning contrary to that attribute, on the model of the contrarieties occurring from black and white, then why do you say that hearing and sight are opposites in this sense? And why is it not permissible to say that blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to see, and deafness is the absence of hearing from that which is supposed to hear? You, in this position, are in need of proving that deafness and blindness are two existent meanings opposite to hearing and sight. The philosophers dispute this most vehemently, for they say: The opposition between sight and blindness and between hearing and deafness is the opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa-l-milka), not the opposition of contraries. And the statement of whoever says: 'Making blindness an absence of sight is not more prior than the reverse' is manifestly false; because if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in our minds and intellects, then this is admitted. However, this yields only that we suspend judgment, and we do not decide on either side. And if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in the matter itself and in reality, then this is denied. Perhaps this priority is realized in the matter itself, even if we do not know the manner of that priority. As for the third premise: That assuming blindness and deafness are opposites in the sense of contrariety, why do you say that every essence capable of the two opposites in this interpretation must necessarily be described with one of them? And why is it not permissible to say that it may be devoid of both? They are required to provide evidence for proving this premise. Then we refute this premise: That air is devoid of all colors and all tastes, and one may not be willing for the actions of the people of immorality nor averse to them. Thus this premise is invalid. As for the fourth premise—which is his statement: 'It is impossible for God Almighty to be described with blindness and deafness, because that is from the attributes of deficiency, and attributes of deficiency are impossible for God Almighty'—they relied in declaring God Almighty free of deficiencies on consensus (ijmāʿ). Then they prove that consensus is a proof by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths. So this proof becomes ultimately scriptural (samʿiyya). Then we see that the apparent meanings indicating that He is hearing and seeing are stronger and more numerous than the apparent meanings indicating that consensus is a proof. And when the matter is so, then for us to adhere in proving that God Almighty is hearing and seeing to these strong apparent meanings, and to drop from ourselves the obligation of articulating these obscure, dark premises, is more appropriate. This is what we say in this chapter.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple premises used by those who argue that God must be described with hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). Razi first allows that God's essence and life might not be incompatible with these attributes, but then questions the necessity of their affirmation. He examines the second premise—that whatever can be described with an attribute must have it or its opposite—by distinguishing two interpretations of 'opposite.' If it means mere absence, then the premise is trivial and does not prove the impossibility of lacking the attribute, which is the very point in dispute. If it means an existent contrary (like black vs. white), then the opponent must prove that deafness and blindness are existent opposites, not merely privations. Razi appeals to the philosophers who hold that the opposition is privation and possession (ʿadam wa-milka), not contrariety. The third premise—that whatever can receive two opposites must have one—is refuted by counterexamples: air has no color or taste, and a person may neither will nor detest certain actions. The fourth premise—that God cannot have deficiency—is based on consensus, which itself relies on scriptural evidence. Razi argues that the scriptural indications for God's hearing and sight are stronger and more numerous, so it is better to rely on them directly rather than on the weak premises presented by the opponents.
