Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something can receive two opposites, it must always have one of them (e.g., an object must be either black or white).
Correction: Counterexamples like air (colorless, tasteless) and human indifference show that a substrate can be devoid of both opposites.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does Razi use as a counterexample to the third premise?
A) Fire is hot
B) Air is colorless and tasteless
C) Water is wet
D) Earth is solid

Key Takeaway
The third premise fails because capability of opposites does not necessitate actual possession of one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you felt you had to have a strong opinion or feeling—either love or hate, approval or disapproval—about something. But is it possible to be neutral, like the air being colorless? What pressure inside you made you think you must choose one side? How can this lesson free you from that false dichotomy? Resolve to examine one such situation this week and consider whether neutrality is permissible.
?
Razi points out that a person may be neither willing nor averse to the actions of immoral people. Reflect: Is there a person or group whose actions you feel you must either condemn or condone? What would it mean to simply not have an active will for or against them? Could that be a form of humility before God's judgment? Challenge yourself to identify one such case and practice emotional neutrality for one day.

The Fourth Premise: Deficiency and Scriptural Evidence
Razi refutes the fourth premise by exposing its circular reliance on consensus and preferring direct scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's circularity argument against the fourth premise and his preference for direct scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
The fourth premise relies on consensus, which itself depends on scriptural evidence weaker than the verses affirming God's hearing and sight.

The final premise is that God cannot be described with deficiency, so He must have hearing and sight. Razi points out that this relies on consensus, which is based on scriptural texts. He then argues that the scriptural texts affirming God's hearing and sight are stronger, making the direct evidence more reliable.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth premise and Razi's response
Read the passage carefully. Notice how Razi traces the argument back to scriptural evidence.
2
Understand the reliance on consensus
The opponent uses consensus to assert that deficiency is impossible for God. But consensus itself is proven as a source by scriptural proof-texts (ẓawāhir).
This makes the entire argument ultimately based on scripture.
3
Compare the strength of scriptural evidence
Razi argues that the scriptural evidence for God's hearing and sight is stronger and more numerous than the evidence for consensus being a proof.
Therefore, it is more rational to rely directly on the stronger evidence.
4
Conclude with Razi's preference
Razi suggests dropping the weak premises and sticking to the clear scriptural proofs for God's attributes.
This is a practical argument for simplicity and strength of evidence.

Source Text

المسألة السادسة عشرة

وأيضًا بتقدير أن لا تكون ذاته المخصوصة وحياته المخصوصة منافية للسمع والبصر لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ثبوتهما مشروط بحصول هذا التأثير، ولما كان هذا الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى كان المشروط أيضًا كذلك ؟ فهذه الاحتمالات قائمة في تحولهم : إنه تعالى يصح أن يكون موصوفًا بالسمع والبصر.

أما المقدمة الثانية وهي قولهم : كل ما يصح اتصافه بصفة فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موصوفًا بتلك الصفة أو بضدها.

فنقول : إن أردتم بضد الصفة عدم الصفة كان المعنى أن كل ما اتصافه صح بصفة فإما أن يكون موصوفا بها وإما أن لا يكون وهذا حق، ولكن لم قلتم إن عدم اتصافه بتلك الصفة محال؟ فإن هذا عين المتنازع فيه ولا يمكن جعله مقدمة في إثبات المتنازع، وإن أردتم بضد الصفة معنى وجوديًا منافيًا لتلك الصفة على وزان المنافيات الحاصلة من السواد والبياض فلم قلتم : إن السمع والبصر ضدان بهذا المعنى ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر عما شأنه أن من يبصر، والصمم عبارة عن عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يسمع ؟ فأنتم في هذا المقام محتاجون إلى إثبات أن الصمم والعمى معنيان موجودان مضادان للسمع والبصر. والفلاسفة ينازعون فيه أشد المنازعة، فإنهم يقولون : تقابل البصر والعمى وتقابل السمع والصمم تقابل العدم والملكة لا تقابل الضدين وقول من قال : ليس جعل العمى عدما للبصر أولى من العكس ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنه إن أريد هذه بعدم الأولوية عدمها في أذهاننا وعقولنا فهذا مسلم إلا أن هذا لا ينتج إلا أن نتوقف فيه، ولا نقطع على أحد الجانبين، وإن أريد بعدم هذه الأولوية عدمها في نفس الأمر وفي الحقيقة فهذا ممنوع، ولعل هذه الأولوية حاصلة في نفس الأمر، وإن كنا لا نعرف كيفية تلك الأولوية. أما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن بتقدير أن يكون العمى والصمم متقابلين تقابل التضاد لم قلتم : إن كل ذات تكون قابلة للضدين بهذا التفسير، فإنه لا بد أن تكون موصوفة بأحدهما ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه قد تكون خالية عنهما ؟ فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدلالة على إثبات هذه المقدمة.

ثم إنا ننقض هذه المقدمة : بأن الهواء خال من جميع الألوان ومن جميع الطعوم، والواحد قد لا يكون مريدا لأفعال أهل الفسوق ولا كارها لها، فبطلت هذه المقدمة.

أما المقدمة الرابعة - وهي قوله : إنه لا يمكن اتصافه تعالى بالعمى والصمم؛ لأن ذلك من صفات النقص، وصفات النقص على الله تعالى محال - أنهم عولوا في تنزيه الله تعالى عن النقائص على الإجماع، ثم إنهم يثبتون كون الإجماع حجة بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث، فصارت هذه الدلالة بالآخرة سمعية، ثم إنا نرى أن الظواهر الدالة على كونه سميعا بصيرًا أقوى من الظواهر الدالة على أن الإجماع حجة وأكثر، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فبأن نتمسك في إثبات كونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بهذه الظواهر القوية، ونسقط عن أنفسنا التزام تقرير هذه المقدمات الخفية المظلمة، كان أولى فهذا ما نقوله في هذا الباب.
The sixteenth issue: And also, assuming that His specific essence and His specific life are not incompatible with hearing and sight, why is it not permissible to say that their affirmation is conditional upon the occurrence of this effect? And since this condition is impossible to obtain in the case of God Almighty, then the conditional is also likewise? So these possibilities stand in their claim that God Almighty can be described with hearing and sight. As for the second premise, which is their statement: 'Everything whose description with an attribute is valid must necessarily be described with that attribute or its opposite.' We say: If you mean by the opposite of the attribute the absence of the attribute, then the meaning is that everything whose description with an attribute is valid is either described with it or not—and this is true. But why do you say that its not being described with that attribute is impossible? For this is the very point of dispute, and it cannot be made a premise in proving the disputed point. And if you mean by the opposite of the attribute an existent meaning contrary to that attribute, on the model of the contrarieties occurring from black and white, then why do you say that hearing and sight are opposites in this sense? And why is it not permissible to say that blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to see, and deafness is the absence of hearing from that which is supposed to hear? You, in this position, are in need of proving that deafness and blindness are two existent meanings opposite to hearing and sight. The philosophers dispute this most vehemently, for they say: The opposition between sight and blindness and between hearing and deafness is the opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa-l-milka), not the opposition of contraries. And the statement of whoever says: 'Making blindness an absence of sight is not more prior than the reverse' is manifestly false; because if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in our minds and intellects, then this is admitted. However, this yields only that we suspend judgment, and we do not decide on either side. And if by this lack of priority is meant its lack in the matter itself and in reality, then this is denied. Perhaps this priority is realized in the matter itself, even if we do not know the manner of that priority. As for the third premise: That assuming blindness and deafness are opposites in the sense of contrariety, why do you say that every essence capable of the two opposites in this interpretation must necessarily be described with one of them? And why is it not permissible to say that it may be devoid of both? They are required to provide evidence for proving this premise. Then we refute this premise: That air is devoid of all colors and all tastes, and one may not be willing for the actions of the people of immorality nor averse to them. Thus this premise is invalid. As for the fourth premise—which is his statement: 'It is impossible for God Almighty to be described with blindness and deafness, because that is from the attributes of deficiency, and attributes of deficiency are impossible for God Almighty'—they relied in declaring God Almighty free of deficiencies on consensus (ijmāʿ). Then they prove that consensus is a proof by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths. So this proof becomes ultimately scriptural (samʿiyya). Then we see that the apparent meanings indicating that He is hearing and seeing are stronger and more numerous than the apparent meanings indicating that consensus is a proof. And when the matter is so, then for us to adhere in proving that God Almighty is hearing and seeing to these strong apparent meanings, and to drop from ourselves the obligation of articulating these obscure, dark premises, is more appropriate. This is what we say in this chapter.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple premises used by those who argue that God must be described with hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). Razi first allows that God's essence and life might not be incompatible with these attributes, but then questions the necessity of their affirmation. He examines the second premise—that whatever can be described with an attribute must have it or its opposite—by distinguishing two interpretations of 'opposite.' If it means mere absence, then the premise is trivial and does not prove the impossibility of lacking the attribute, which is the very point in dispute. If it means an existent contrary (like black vs. white), then the opponent must prove that deafness and blindness are existent opposites, not merely privations. Razi appeals to the philosophers who hold that the opposition is privation and possession (ʿadam wa-milka), not contrariety. The third premise—that whatever can receive two opposites must have one—is refuted by counterexamples: air has no color or taste, and a person may neither will nor detest certain actions. The fourth premise—that God cannot have deficiency—is based on consensus, which itself relies on scriptural evidence. Razi argues that the scriptural indications for God's hearing and sight are stronger and more numerous, so it is better to rely on them directly rather than on the weak premises presented by the opponents.

Key Terms
الإجماع
ظواهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that God cannot be deficient is an independent rational proof.
Correction: It ultimately rests on consensus and scriptural evidence, and the scriptural evidence for God's attributes is stronger.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the fourth premise ultimately reliant on scriptural evidence?
A) Because the opponent uses consensus, which itself is proven by scripture.
B) Because the opponent uses reason alone.
C) Because the opponent uses empirical observation.
D) Because the opponent uses intuition.

Key Takeaway
When scriptural evidence for God's attributes is clear and strong, it is better to rely on it than on weak philosophical premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'He is the Hearing, the Seeing,' do you accept them with full certainty, or do you ever allow philosophical doubts to weaken your conviction? What would it take for you to prioritize Allah's words over human reasoning?
?
Razi warns that obscure premises can lead to darkness. Are there any beliefs you hold simply because they are widely accepted, without personally verifying them against the Quran and Sunnah? How can you change that starting today?
?
The opponents made a valid point about deficiency attributes, but their method was flawed. Have you ever defended a correct belief using weak arguments, only to later find stronger evidence? This lesson calls you to base your faith on the strongest foundation: revelation. What step will you take to ensure your knowledge of Allah comes directly from His Book?

First Objection of the Deniers and Its Answer
Razi presents the deniers' first argument and answers it, then faces a counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' first objection against God's hearing and sight and the response that distinguishes between dispositional attributes and actual perception.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight are unchanging dispositions; change occurs in perceived objects only.

Razi begins his defense of the divine attributes by presenting and refuting the first objection of those who deny that God hears and sees. The objection claims that if God had these senses, they must be eternal or originated — both problematic. Razi's answer introduces the concept of dispositional attributes (ṣifat mustaʿidda) that do not change. However, a counter-objection challenges this distinction, leaving a point for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two horns
Identify the deniers' argument: if God has hearing/sight, they must be eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). Both are argued to be impossible. Note the reasoning for each horn.
The first horn involves seeing the nonexistent; the second horn makes God a locus of events.
2
Understand the answer's key distinction
Razi answers that hearing and sight are dispositional attributes (mustaʿidda) for perception when objects exist. Change occurs in the perceived objects, not in the attributes themselves.
This avoids the problem of change in God while maintaining real perception.
3
Consider the counter-objection
An interlocutor raises that if perception depends on the presence of objects, then the act of perceiving is distinct from the disposition, implying a renewed attribute in God. Reflect on why this challenges the answer.
The text does not provide Razi's further response here; this is an unresolved point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المنكرون لكونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بوجهين :

الشبهة الأولى : لو كان الله تعالى سميعا بصيرًا لكان سمعه وبصره إما أن يكون قديما أو محدثًا والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً؛ إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قديما ؛ لأن العالم كان معدوماً في الأزل، ورؤية المعدوم وسمع المعدوم محال، وإن التزم جاهل أن يكون المعدوم مرئيا ومسموعا فنقول: إنه تعالى يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوماً ؛ إذ لو رآه موجوداً لكان ذلك غلطا وجهلًا، وهو على الله تعالى محال، ثم إذا وجد العالم فلا بد وأن يراه موجودا، وإلا عاد حديث الغلط، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم التبدل والتغير وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون سمعه وبصره محدثًا لأنه لو كان كذلك لصار محلا للحوادث، وهو محال.

والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن السمع والبصر صفتان مستعدتان لإدراك المسموعات والمبصرات عند وجودها، فالتغير يقع في المسموع والمبصر لا في السمع والبصر. ولقائل أن يقول : أليس أن كون السمع والبصر مدركين للمسموع والمبصر، موقوف على حضور المبصر والمسموع ؟ وهذا الإدراك الذي هو موقوف على حضور المسموع والمبصر مغايرا لتلك الصفة التي هي غير موقوفة على حضور المسموع والمبصر، فيلزمكم أن يكون كون الله تعالى مدركًا للمسموعات والمبصرات صفة متجددة.
The deniers of God Almighty being hearing and seeing argued with two aspects: The first doubt: If God Almighty were hearing and seeing, then His hearing and sight would either be eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath), and both divisions are invalid. Therefore, the statement that God Almighty is hearing and seeing is invalid. We say: It is not permissible that they be eternal because the world was nonexistent in pre-eternity, and seeing the nonexistent and hearing the nonexistent are impossible. And if an ignorant person insists that the nonexistent can be seen and heard, we say: He Almighty sees the world at the time of its nonexistence as nonexistent; for if He saw it as existent, that would be error and ignorance, which is impossible for God Almighty. Then when the world comes into existence, He must see it as existent, otherwise the matter of error returns. On this assumption, change and alteration would follow. We say: It is not permissible that His hearing and sight be originated because if that were the case, He would become a locus for originated events (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith), which is impossible. The answer to the first doubt: That hearing and sight are two dispositional attributes (ṣifatān mustaʿiddatān) for perceiving audible and visible things when they exist. So change occurs in the audible and visible, not in hearing and sight. And someone may say: Is it not that the hearing and sight being perceivers of the audible and visible is dependent on the presence of the visible and audible? And this perception, which is dependent on the presence of the audible and visible, is different from that attribute which is not dependent on the presence of the audible and visible. So it follows for you that God Almighty’s being a perceiver of audibles and visibles is a renewed attribute (ṣifat mutajaddida).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubha) of those who deny that God has hearing and sight, along with the author's answer and a further counter-objection. The objection argues that if God had hearing and sight, they must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, they would involve perception of nonexistent things (since the world was not yet created) or would require change when the world appears. If originated, God would be a locus of temporal events, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between the attributes of hearing and sight as dispositional (mustaʿidda) and the actual perception. The attributes themselves are unchanging; change occurs only in the objects perceived. The opponent then raises a counter: if perception depends on the presence of the object, then the act of perceiving is distinct from the disposition, and thus it would be a renewed attribute in God, implying change. This counter is left unanswered in the text, as the author moves to the second objection.

Key Terms
شبهة
قديم
محدث
رؤية المعدوم
تبدل
صفة مستعدة
إدراك متجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that God cannot have hearing and sight without change or composition.
Correction: Razi's answer shows that by distinguishing dispositional attributes from actual perception, change is not in God but in the objects perceived. However, the counter-objection remains.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, where does the change occur?
A) In the divine attributes themselves
B) In the objects perceived (the audible and visible)
C) In the divine essence
D) Nowhere; change is impossible

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are unchanging dispositional attributes that enable perception of objects when they exist, avoiding change in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing is an unchanging disposition, always ready to hear, what does it mean for you that He heard every word you said today—including those you wish you hadn't? How will you change what you say tomorrow?
?
The deniers thought God's sight would require seeing the nonexistent. But we learned His sight is a constant attribute. When you sin in secret, do you truly believe God sees you? What would it take for that belief to govern your actions?
?
The counter-objection suggests that if perception depends on an object, then God's perception is renewed. But we affirm that His attributes are not dependent. Does your prayer life reflect a God whose hearing is constant, or do you pray as if He only hears when you are 'good enough'?

Divine Attributes

First Objection: Are God's Hearing and Sight Eternal or Originated?
Razi presents and answers the first objection against divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against God's attributes of hearing and sight, and how Razi defends the doctrine by distinguishing between stable attributes and change in perceived objects.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight are stable dispositions, not changing perceptions.

The deniers of God's attributes of hearing and sight put forward a dilemma: either these attributes are eternal or originated, both of which they claim are impossible. Razi responds by clarifying that hearing and sight are inherent qualities (ṣifāt) that are disposed to perceive when objects exist; change occurs in the objects, not in the attributes. This lesson presents the full objection and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the dilemma: if God is Hearing and Seeing, His hearing and sight must be either eternal or originated, both considered impossible.
2
Analyze the two sub-arguments
Identify why eternal hearing/sight is impossible (would perceive non-existence) and why originated hearing/sight is impossible (would make God a locus for temporal events).
The objection uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine Razi's answer
Razi argues that hearing and sight are stable qualities (ṣifāt mustaʿidda) disposed to perceive when objects exist. Change happens in the objects, not in the divine attributes.
This preserves God's immutability.
4
Note the unresolved sub-objection
A hypothetical objector raises that if perception depends on the presence of objects, then the act of perceiving is a renewed quality. Razi does not answer here; consider why the same principle might apply.
This indicates a lacuna in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المنكرون لكونه تعالى سميعا بصيرا بوجهين : الشبهة الأولى : لو كان الله تعالى سميعا بصيرًا لكان سمعه وبصره إما أن يكون قديما أو محدثًا والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً؛ إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قديما ؛ لأن العالم كان معدوماً في الأزل، ورؤية المعدوم وسمع المعدوم محال، وإن التزم جاهل أن يكون المعدوم مرئيا ومسموعا فنقول: إنه تعالى يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوماً ؛ إذ لو رآه موجوداً لكان ذلك غلطا وجهلًا، وهو على الله تعالى محال، ثم إذا وجد العالم فلا بد وأن يراه موجودا، وإلا عاد حديث الغلط، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم التبدل والتغير وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون سمعه وبصره محدثًا لأنه لو كان كذلك لصار محلا للحوادث، وهو محال. والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن السمع والبصر صفتان مستعدتان لإدراك المسموعات والمبصرات عند وجودها، فالتغير يقع في المسموع والمبصر لا في السمع والبصر. ولقائل أن يقول : أليس أن كون السمع والبصر مدركين للمسموع والمبصر، موقوف على حضور المبصر والمسموع ؟ وهذا الإدراك الذي هو موقوف على حضور المسموع والمبصر مغايرا لتلك الصفة التي هي غير موقوفة على حضور المسموع والمبصر، فيلزمكم أن يكون كون الله تعالى مدركًا للمسموعات والمبصرات صفة متجددة.
And the deniers argued against His being the Exalted Hearing and Seeing by two aspects: The first doubt: If Allah the Exalted were Hearing and Seeing, then His hearing and seeing must be either eternal or originated, and both divisions are false. Therefore, the statement that He is Hearing and Seeing is false. We say: It cannot be eternal because the world was non-existent in pre-eternity, and seeing non-existence and hearing non-existence are impossible. Even if an ignorant person insists that non-existence can be seen and heard, we say: He sees the world at the time of its non-existence as non-existent; for if He saw it as existent, that would be error and ignorance, which is impossible for Allah the Exalted. Then when the world comes into existence, He must see it as existent, otherwise the error recurs. On this basis, change and alteration would follow. And we say: It cannot be that His hearing and seeing are originated, because if so, He would become a locus for originated things, which is impossible. The answer to the first doubt: Hearing and seeing are two qualities (ṣifatān) disposed to perceive audible and visible things when they exist. So change occurs in the audible and visible, not in the hearing and seeing. And someone may say: Is it not that the hearing and seeing being perceivers of the audible and visible depends on the presence of the visible and audible? And this perception which depends on the presence of the audible and visible is different from that quality which does not depend on the presence of the audible and visible. Therefore, it follows for you that Allah's being a perceiver of audibles and visibles is a renewed quality.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against God's attributes of hearing and sight, along with Razi's answer and a further rejoinder. The deniers argue that if God were Hearing and Seeing, His hearing and sight would have to be either eternal or originated. They claim both are impossible. If eternal, then they would have existed before the world, and perceiving non-existence is impossible. If originated, then God would become a locus for temporal events, which is also impossible. Razi answers by clarifying that hearing and sight are stable qualities (ṣifatān) disposed to perceive when the objects exist. Change occurs in the perceived objects, not in the qualities themselves. A hypothetical objector then raises a further problem: if perception is dependent on the presence of the object, then the act of perceiving is a distinct, renewed attribute, implying change in God. Razi does not provide an explicit answer to this sub-objection in the text, indicating a possible lacuna or assumption that the same principle applies.

Key Terms
شبهة
قديم
محدث
السمع والبصر
صفات مستعدة
إدراك
تبدل وتغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's answer implies that God's attributes change when objects appear.
Correction: Razi insists that change occurs in the objects, not in the hearing and sight themselves, which are stable dispositions.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, what is the problem with saying God's hearing and sight are eternal?
A) It would make God dependent on created things.
B) They would perceive non-existence, which is impossible.
C) It would imply God is a body.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are eternal dispositions, not acts that change with time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s hearing and sight are eternal dispositions that do not depend on when you speak or act, how does that change the way you approach Him in your private moments? What secret thoughts or actions are you now more aware that He perceives, and what will you do about that awareness?
?
Razi distinguishes between the attribute (hearing) and the perception of a particular sound. When you pray, do you believe you are connecting to a God whose hearing is constant, or do you sometimes feel He 'hears' only at that moment? How can you train yourself to live with the constant awareness that His hearing and sight are never turned off?
?
The deniers argued that if God sees the world as existent after it was non-existent, change would be required in His sight. Razi replied that change is in the object, not the attribute. How does this truth give you security in God’s unchangeability, and does it challenge any doubts you may have about God’s responsiveness to your changing circumstances?

Second Objection Against God's Hearing and Sight and Its Answer
Razi presents and answers the second objection to divine hearing/sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that God's hearing and sight require sense organs by distinguishing between constant conjunction and necessary condition.

Core Idea
Correlation does not prove causation; God's attributes transcend worldly patterns.

The second objection against God's attributes of hearing and sight claims that these perceptions depend on sense organs, which are physical and thus incompatible with God's incorporeality. Razi's response is a masterclass in logical reasoning: he points out that we often mistake correlation for necessary condition. He illustrates this with the example of life and power, which in humans depend on a bodily temperament, yet we affirm them for God without any body. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice its core claim: hearing/sight require sense organs, therefore impossible for God.
2
Identify Razi's key move
Razi challenges the proof: the objector assumes that because we always observe hearing with sense organs, the sense organ is a necessary condition. Razi denies that evidence.
The distinction is between 'iqtiran' (conjunction) and 'ishtirat' (condition).
3
Analyze the supporting example
Razi uses life and power as analogies: they always occur with temperament in humans, but we affirm them for God without temperament. This shows that mere conjunction does not prove conditionality.
The example strengthens the logical point by showing it is accepted in another context.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends God's attributes without needing to explain the mechanism. It shifts the burden of proof back to the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية: السمع والبصر لا يحصلان إلا مع تأثير الحاسة، وذلك من صفات الأجسام، وهو على الله تعالى محال. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أنكم ما ذكرتم دليلا على أن الإبصار والسماع مشروطان بحصول تأثير الحاسة، بل نجد في الشاهد أن الإبصار والسماع لا يحصلان إلا عند هذا التأثير، ولكن مجرد الاقتران لا يدل على الاشتراط، بدليل أن الحياة والقدرة لا يحصلان إلا عند المزاج، ثم إنا نثبتهما في حق الله تعالى مع القطع بكونه تعالى منزها عن الجسمية والمزاج، فعلمنا أن مجرد المقارنة لا تدل على الاشتراط.
The second doubt: Hearing and sight do not occur except with the effect of the sense organ (ḥāssah), and that is among the attributes of bodies, and that is impossible for Allah the Exalted. The answer to the second doubt: You have not mentioned any proof that sight and hearing are conditioned upon the occurrence of the effect of the sense organ. Rather, we find in the witnessed world that sight and hearing do not occur except upon this effect, but mere conjunction (mujarrad al-iqtirān) does not indicate conditioning (ishtirāṭ), as evidence that life and power do not occur except upon temperament (mizāj), yet we affirm them with respect to Allah the Exalted while being certain that He is exalted above corporeality and temperament. So we know that mere correlation does not indicate conditioning.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to God's attributes of hearing and sight and Razi's answer. The objection is that hearing and sight are only possible through the effect of a sense organ (such as an ear or eye), which is a property of physical bodies, and thus cannot be attributed to a non-corporeal God. Razi answers by pointing out that the objectors have not provided a proof that the effect of a sense organ is a necessary condition (sharṭ) for hearing and sight. He distinguishes between constant conjunction and necessary condition: just because we observe that hearing and sight always occur with sense organs in this world does not prove that the sense organ is a necessary condition. He provides an analogy: life and power are always observed with a specific bodily temperament (mizāj) in human beings, yet Muslims affirm that God has life and power without any body or temperament. This shows that mere correlation does not prove a necessary causal relationship. Therefore, the objection fails.

الاقتران
الاشتراط
المزاج
الحياة
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because hearing and sight always require sense organs in our experience, they must always require them, so God cannot have them.
Correction: Razi shows that constant conjunction does not prove a necessary condition; life and power always accompany temperament in humans but are affirmed for God without temperament.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the claim that hearing and sight require a sense organ?
A) By arguing that sense organs are not needed for perception.
B) By stating that constant conjunction does not prove a necessary condition, using life and power as examples.
C) By denying that hearing and sight are real attributes of God.
D) By saying that God's hearing and sight are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God cannot have hearing/sight because they require sense organs is refuted by distinguishing between correlation and necessary condition, as illustrated by the example of life and power.

?
Razi uses the example of life and power: you cannot see God's life and power in creation without a body, yet you affirm them. Is your faith in God's attributes truly based on revelation, or on what you can conceive from your own existence? Are you secretly trying to fit God into your mental box? Write down one attribute of God that you struggle to grasp and pray for trust in His revealed description.
?

Mere Conjunction Does Not Imply Conditionality
Al-Razi argues that mere conjunction does not prove conditionality, using an analogy with life and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere conjunction (correlation) does not prove conditionality, using al-Razi's analogy of life and power.

Core Idea
Correlation does not equal conditionality, even in theology.

Al-Razi tackles a key methodological principle: just because two things occur together in the created world does not mean one is a necessary condition for the other. He illustrates this with the example of life and power, which in humans require a physical temperament but are attributed to God without any physicality. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the terms 'اقتران' and 'اشتراط'.
2
Analyze the analogy
Notice that al-Razi uses life and power as examples: they require temperament in humans but not in God. This shows that conjunction does not imply conditionality.
The argument is analogical: if you can separate life from temperament in the divine case, you can separate sight from sense organs.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this principle protects God's transcendence (tanzih). It establishes that divine attributes are not bound by physical conditions observed in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشروطان بحصول تأثير الحاسة، بل نجد في الشاهد أن الإبصار والسماع لا يحصلان إلا عند هذا التأثير، ولكن مجرد الاقتران لا يدل على الاشتراط، بدليل أن الحياة والقدرة لا يحصلان إلا عند المزاج، ثم إنا نثبتهما في حق الله تعالى مع القطع بكونه تعالى منزها عن الجسمية والمزاج، فعلمنا أن مجرد المقارنة لا تدل على الاشتراط.
Both are conditioned on the occurrence of the effect of the sense; rather, we find in the witnessed realm that sight and hearing do not occur except with this effect, but mere conjunction does not indicate conditionality, as evidenced by the fact that life and power do not occur except with a temperament, yet we affirm them with respect to God Almighty while being certain that He is exalted above corporeality and temperament. Thus we know that mere conjunction does not indicate conditionality.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a short argument concluding the discussion of a previous issue (Mas'ala 16). The author, al-Razi, argues against the claim that the fact that sight and hearing only occur when the sense organ is affected (e.g., the eye being struck by light) implies that the sense must be affected for God to perceive. He uses an analogy: life and power in humans only occur with a specific temperament (mizaj), but we affirm them for God without any corporeality or temperament. Thus, mere conjunction does not prove conditionality; correlation does not imply causation. The argument is analogical and serves to defend divine transcendence (tanzih).

Correction: Al-Razi refutes this by analogy: life and power require temperament in humans, but are affirmed for God without it. Thus, mere conjunction does not prove conditionality.

D) Conjunction is always a sign of conditionality in the divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Mere conjunction (correlation) does not prove conditionality; divine attributes are not limited by physical conditions.

Four Proofs That Speech Is an Inner Reality
Al-Razi enumerates four proofs for inner speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's four proofs that the essence of speech is an inner meaning (kalam nafsi) distinct from uttered words.

Core Idea
Speech is not mere words; its reality is the inner meaning.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles the nature of speech (kalam), a key topic in Islamic theology. He argues that before we even speak, there is an inner request or meaning in the soul. To prove this, he presents four compelling proofs that distinguish the inner reality from the external expressions. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview. Notice how al-Razi introduces the issue and then lists four proofs.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the first two proofs
The first proof focuses on the unchanging nature of the inner request versus changing expressions. The second proof highlights that the uttered command is an indicator (dalil) of the inner request, and the indicator is distinct from the indicated.
These proofs appeal to universality and logical distinction.
3
Understand the last two proofs
The third proof contrasts conventional meaning (uttered words) with essential meaning (inner request). The fourth proof shows that while words can be swapped by convention, the essential natures of request and news cannot be swapped.
These proofs emphasize the inherent reality of inner meanings.
4
Summarize the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that these proofs show the reality of request and news are distinct from utterances. Reflect on how this supports the Ash'ari doctrine of kalam nafsi.
This distinction is crucial for understanding God's attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة عشرة: في كونه تعالى متكلما. والكلام في هذه المسألة مرتب على فصلين: الفصل الأول: في حقيقة الكلام. اعلم أن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يقول: اسقني الماء فإنه قبل أن يتلفظ بهذا اللفظ يجد في نفسه طلبًا واقتضاء لذلك الفعل وماهية ذلك الطلب مغايرة لذلك اللفظ. والذي يدل عليه وجوه: الأول: أن ماهية ذلك الطلب لا تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة، والألفاظ الدالة على هذا المعنى تختلف باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة. الثاني: أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل "افعل" دليل على جميع ذلك الطلب القائم بالقلب، ولا شك أن الدليل مغاير للمدلول. الثالث: أن جميع العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل "افعل" لا يكون طلبًا وأمرًا إلا عند اصطلاح الناس على هذا الموضوع، وأما كون ذلك المعنى القائم بالقلب طلبًا فإنه أمر ذاتي حقيقي لا يحتاج فيه إلى الوضع والاصطلاح. الرابع: وهو أنهم قالوا: إن قولنا: ضرب يضرب إخبار، وقولنا: اضرب، لا تضرب، أمر ونهي. ولو أن الواضعين قلبوا الأمر وقالوا: إن قولنا ضرب يضرب أمر ونهي وقولنا اضرب لا تضرب إخبار لكان ذلك ممكنا جائزا. أما لو قالوا: حقيقة الطلب يمكن أن تنقلب خبرًا، وحقيقة الخبر يمكن أن تنقلب طلبًا لكان ذلك محالاً. فهذه الوجوه الظاهرة دالة على أن حقيقة الطلب وحقيقة الخبر أمر مغاير لهذه الألفاظ، وهذه العبارات، بل هذه الألفاظ وهذه العبارات دالة عليها معرفة لها.
The seventeenth issue: On His being a speaker. The discussion on this issue is arranged into two chapters: First chapter: On the reality of speech. Know that when a person wants to say 'Give me water', before he utters this utterance, he finds within himself a request and a demand for that action, and the essence of that request is distinct from that utterance. What indicates this are aspects: First: That the essence of that request does not change with the variation of times and places, while the expressions indicating this meaning vary with variation of times and places. Second: That rational people know necessarily that the statement of the speaker 'Do' is an indication of all that request residing in the heart, and without doubt the indicator is distinct from the indicated. Third: That all rational people know necessarily that the statement of the speaker 'Do' is not a request and command except by convention of people on this usage, whereas that meaning residing in the heart being a request is a real, essential matter that does not require convention and usage. Fourth: That they said: Our saying 'daraba yadribu' is news, and our saying 'idrib' and 'la tadrib' are command and prohibition. If the coiners had reversed the matter and said that our saying 'daraba yadribu' is command and prohibition and our saying 'idrib' and 'la tadrib' is news, that would have been possible and permissible. But if they said that the reality of request can turn into news and the reality of news can turn into request, that would be impossible. So these evident aspects indicate that the reality of request and the reality of news are distinct from these utterances and expressions; rather these utterances and expressions are indicative of them and make them known.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Mas'ala 17, which addresses God's attribute of speech (kalam). Al-Razi first divides the discussion into two chapters; here he gives the first chapter on the reality of speech. He asserts that there is an inner speech (kalam nafsi) distinct from uttered expressions, using the example of a person who wants to say 'Give me water'—before speaking, there is an inner request. He then presents four proofs (wujuh) for this distinction: (1) The inner request does not change with time/place, unlike the words that express it. (2) The uttered command ('Do') is an indicator (dalil) of the inner request; the indicator is distinct from the indicated. (3) The uttered command only means command by convention, but the inner request is inherently a request without need for convention. (4) Words can be swapped in meaning by convention (e.g., a word for news could be used for command), but the essential nature of request and news cannot be swapped—they are real and distinct. Therefore, the reality of speech is the inner meaning, not the spoken words. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine of kalam nafsi as an eternal attribute of God.

طلب
خبر
حقيقة
لفظ
دلالة
مدلول
اصطلاح
ذاتي
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That speech is only the uttered words (lafdh) and has no inner reality.
Correction: Al-Razi proves that there is an inner meaning (ma'na) that is the true reality of speech, and uttered words are merely indicators.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what is the relationship between the uttered command 'Do' and the inner request?
A) They are identical.
B) The uttered command is an indicator of the inner request, and they are distinct.
C) The inner request depends on the uttered command for its existence.
D) The uttered command is the cause of the inner request.

Key Takeaway
Inner speech is the true reality of kalam, distinct from uttered words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you ever pause and realize that your intention or meaning existed before the words? How does this awareness change your understanding of your own speech and its sincerity?
?
If inner speech is the true reality, how accountable are you for the meanings in your heart, even if you never utter them? What does this imply about your inner thoughts before Allah?
?
Al-Razi says words can be swapped by convention, but the inner reality cannot. Is there an area in your life where you rely on outward expressions (like saying 'Alhamdulillah') without the inner state? How can you align the two?

Four Proofs that Inner Speech is Distinct from Utterances
Razi enumerates four proofs that inner demand differs from uttered words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s four arguments proving that the inner demand in the soul is distinct from spoken words.

Core Idea
Inner speech is real and distinct from uttered words; words are just signs.

Razi begins by noting that before we speak, we find a demand within ourselves. He then presents four compelling proofs that this inner meaning is not the same as the sounds we utter. This lesson unpacks those proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening observation
Notice how Razi uses a simple example (asking for water) to point to an inner demand that exists before speech.
This sets the stage for all four proofs.
2
Study each proof in order
Go through proof 1 (invariance), proof 2 (sign-signified distinction), proof 3 (convention vs. reality), and proof 4 (possibility of swapping words but not essences).
Each proof highlights a different angle: temporal, logical, conventional, and essential.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
After the four proofs, Razi concludes that the reality of demand and informative is different from the words. Understand why this is a strong case.
The conclusion uses the phrase 'بل هذه الألفاظ دالة عليها'.
4
Test your understanding
Try to restate each proof in your own words and see if you can identify which proof addresses which aspect.
This will solidify the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يقول : اسقني الماء فإنه قبل أن يتلفظ بهذا اللفظ يجد في نفسه طلبًا واقتضاء لذلك الفعل وماهية ذلك الطلب مغايرة لذلك اللفظ والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول: أن ماهية ذلك الطلب لا تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة، والألفاظ الدالة على هذا المعنى تختلف باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة. الثاني : أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل "افعل " دليل على ذلك الطلب القائم بالقلب، ولا شك أن الدليل مغاير للمدلول. الثالث : أن جميع العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل " افعل " لا يكون طلبا وأمرًا إلا عند اصطلاح الناس على هذا الموضوع، وأما كون ذلك المعنى القائم بالقلب طلبا فإنه أمر ذاتي حقيقي لا يحتاج فيه إلى الوضع والاصطلاح. الرابع : وهو أنهم قالوا: إن قولنا : ضرب يضرب إخبار، وقولنا : اضرب، لا تضرب، أمر ونهي ولو أن الواضعين قلبوا الأمر وقالوا : إن قولنا ضرب يضرب أمر ونهي وقولنا اضرب لا تضرب إخبار لكان ذلك ممكنا جائزا . أما لو قالوا : حقيقة الطلب يمكن أن تنقلب خبرًا، وحقيقة الخبر يمكن أن تنقلب طلبًا لكان ذلك محالاً. فهذه الوجوه الظاهرة دالة على أن حقيقة الطلب وحقيقة الخبر أمر مغاير لهذه الألفاظ، وهذه العبارات، بل هذه الألفاظ وهذه العبارات دالة عليها معرفة لها. إذا عرفت هذا فلنبحث عن ماهية هذا الطلب وماهية الحكم الذهني الذي يسمى بالخبر، فنقول : هذا الطلب إما أن يكون هو الإرادة وإما أن يكون معنى مغايرا الإرادة، والأول باطل، فتعين الثاني، وهو المطلوب.
Know that when a person wants to say: 'Give me water,' before he utters this word, he finds within himself a demand (ṭalab) and a requisition (iqtiḍā') for that action, and the essence (māhiyya) of that demand is different from that utterance. That which indicates it are aspects (wujūh): First: that the essence of that demand does not change with the difference of times and places, while the words indicating this meaning differ with the difference of times and places. Second: that rational people know by necessity that the speaker's saying 'do' is evidence (dalīl) for that demand subsisting in the heart, and there is no doubt that the evidence is different from the evidenced. Third: that all rational people know by necessity that the speaker's saying 'do' is only a demand and command by convention of people upon this designation, whereas the subsistence of that meaning in the heart as a demand is an essential, real matter not requiring designation and convention. Fourth: that they said: our saying 'ḍaraba yaḍribu' is informative (khabar), and our saying 'iḍrib' and 'lā taḍrib' is command and prohibition. If the language inventors reversed the matter and said that our saying 'ḍaraba yaḍribu' is command and prohibition and our saying 'iḍrib' and 'lā taḍrib' is informative, that would be possible and permissible. But if they said: 'the reality of demand can be transformed into informative, and the reality of informative can be transformed into demand,' that would be impossible. So these evident aspects indicate that the reality of demand and the reality of informative are something different from these words and expressions; rather these words and expressions are evidence for them and make them known. When you understand this, let us investigate the essence of this demand and the essence of the mental judgment which is called informative (khabar). We say: this demand is either the will (irāda) or a meaning different from will. The first is false, so the second is established, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a set of four arguments (wujūh) proving that the inner speech (the demand found in the soul) is distinct from the uttered words. Razi begins by observing that when a person intends to say 'Give me water,' he finds within himself a demand or requisition for that action before any utterance. The essence of that inner demand is different from the word. He then gives four proofs: (1) The inner demand does not change with time or place, whereas the words used to express it vary. (2) The spoken word is a sign or evidence pointing to the inner demand; the evidence is necessarily different from what it evidences. (3) The spoken command is only a command by human convention, but the inner demand is a real, essential thing independent of convention. (4) If language inventors swapped the meanings of words, it would be possible: they could assign informative words to commands and vice versa. But they cannot change the real nature of demand into informative or vice versa; that is impossible. Therefore, the reality of demand and the reality of informative are distinct from the words. Razi then transitions: after establishing that inner speech is different from outward speech, we investigate its essence: is it the same as will (irāda) or something else? He asserts the first is false, so it must be something else. This sets up the next arguments.

Key Terms
طلب
اقتضاء
دليل
مدلول
وضع
اصطلاح
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech is only the uttered sound, and inner thoughts are not considered speech.
Correction: Razi proves there is a real inner demand that is the essence of command, distinct from the word.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the idea that words can be swapped by convention but the underlying natures cannot be altered?
A) Proof 1 (invariance)
B) Proof 2 (sign and signified)
C) Proof 3 (convention vs. reality)
D) Proof 4 (swapping words)

Key Takeaway
The inner demand is distinct from utterances, as shown by four rational proofs, establishing that speech has both inner and outer aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak words of faith, is there a corresponding inner demand within your heart? Tonight, examine your prayers: are they mere utterances or do they arise from a genuine ṭalab? Commit to praying with your heart before your tongue.
?
Recall a moment you gave a command or made a promise. Did your inner state match your words? If not, what held you back? This week, make it a discipline to only speak when your inner demand aligns with your utterance.
?
The words you use are conventional signs—what does that mean for the sincerity of your speech? Think of a recent conversation where your words did not reflect your inner reality. Resolve to be truthful in your next three interactions, ensuring your inner demand matches your expression.

Is Inner Demand the Same as Will?
Razi presents a dichotomy and asserts inner demand is not will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s claim that the inner demand is not identical to the will, but a distinct meaning.

Core Idea
Inner demand is not will; it is something else.

After proving that inner speech is distinct from uttered words, Razi turns to its essence. He proposes two possibilities: it is will (irāda) or something else. He asserts the first is false, without giving proof here, and concludes it is something else. This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy
Razi presents a dichotomy: inner demand is either the will or something else. Recognize that this is a standard kalam method of division.
The dichotomy exhausts the possibilities.
2
Note the claim and its implications
Razi states that the first option (it is will) is false, so the second must be true. Notice that he does not provide the proof here; it will come later.
This sets up further discussion on the nature of inner speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يقول : اسقني الماء فإنه قبل أن يتلفظ بهذا اللفظ يجد في نفسه طلبًا واقتضاء لذلك الفعل وماهية ذلك الطلب مغايرة لذلك اللفظ والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول: أن ماهية ذلك الطلب لا تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة، والألفاظ الدالة على هذا المعنى تختلف باختلاف الأزمنة والأمكنة. الثاني : أن العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل "افعل " دليل على ذلك الطلب القائم بالقلب، ولا شك أن الدليل مغاير للمدلول. الثالث : أن جميع العقلاء يعلمون بالضرورة أن قول القائل " افعل " لا يكون طلبا وأمرًا إلا عند اصطلاح الناس على هذا الموضوع، وأما كون ذلك المعنى القائم بالقلب طلبا فإنه أمر ذاتي حقيقي لا يحتاج فيه إلى الوضع والاصطلاح. الرابع : وهو أنهم قالوا: إن قولنا : ضرب يضرب إخبار، وقولنا : اضرب، لا تضرب، أمر ونهي ولو أن الواضعين قلبوا الأمر وقالوا : إن قولنا ضرب يضرب أمر ونهي وقولنا اضرب لا تضرب إخبار لكان ذلك ممكنا جائزا . أما لو قالوا : حقيقة الطلب يمكن أن تنقلب خبرًا، وحقيقة الخبر يمكن أن تنقلب طلبًا لكان ذلك محالاً. فهذه الوجوه الظاهرة دالة على أن حقيقة الطلب وحقيقة الخبر أمر مغاير لهذه الألفاظ، وهذه العبارات، بل هذه الألفاظ وهذه العبارات دالة عليها معرفة لها. إذا عرفت هذا فلنبحث عن ماهية هذا الطلب وماهية الحكم الذهني الذي يسمى بالخبر، فنقول : هذا الطلب إما أن يكون هو الإرادة وإما أن يكون معنى مغايرا الإرادة، والأول باطل، فتعين الثاني، وهو المطلوب.
Know that when a person wants to say: 'Give me water,' before he utters this word, he finds within himself a demand (ṭalab) and a requisition (iqtiḍā') for that action, and the essence (māhiyya) of that demand is different from that utterance. That which indicates it are aspects (wujūh): First: that the essence of that demand does not change with the difference of times and places, while the words indicating this meaning differ with the difference of times and places. Second: that rational people know by necessity that the speaker's saying 'do' is evidence (dalīl) for that demand subsisting in the heart, and there is no doubt that the evidence is different from the evidenced. Third: that all rational people know by necessity that the speaker's saying 'do' is only a demand and command by convention of people upon this designation, whereas the subsistence of that meaning in the heart as a demand is an essential, real matter not requiring designation and convention. Fourth: that they said: our saying 'ḍaraba yaḍribu' is informative (khabar), and our saying 'iḍrib' and 'lā taḍrib' is command and prohibition. If the language inventors reversed the matter and said that our saying 'ḍaraba yaḍribu' is command and prohibition and our saying 'iḍrib' and 'lā taḍrib' is informative, that would be possible and permissible. But if they said: 'the reality of demand can be transformed into informative, and the reality of informative can be transformed into demand,' that would be impossible. So these evident aspects indicate that the reality of demand and the reality of informative are something different from these words and expressions; rather these words and expressions are evidence for them and make them known. When you understand this, let us investigate the essence of this demand and the essence of the mental judgment which is called informative (khabar). We say: this demand is either the will (irāda) or a meaning different from will. The first is false, so the second is established, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a set of four arguments (wujūh) proving that the inner speech (the demand found in the soul) is distinct from the uttered words. Razi begins by observing that when a person intends to say 'Give me water,' he finds within himself a demand or requisition for that action before any utterance. The essence of that inner demand is different from the word. He then gives four proofs: (1) The inner demand does not change with time or place, whereas the words used to express it vary. (2) The spoken word is a sign or evidence pointing to the inner demand; the evidence is necessarily different from what it evidences. (3) The spoken command is only a command by human convention, but the inner demand is a real, essential thing independent of convention. (4) If language inventors swapped the meanings of words, it would be possible: they could assign informative words to commands and vice versa. But they cannot change the real nature of demand into informative or vice versa; that is impossible. Therefore, the reality of demand and the reality of informative are distinct from the words. Razi then transitions: after establishing that inner speech is different from outward speech, we investigate its essence: is it the same as will (irāda) or something else? He asserts the first is false, so it must be something else. This sets up the next arguments.

Key Terms
إرادة
طلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the inner demand for an action is the same as the will to do that action.
Correction: Razi separates them: the inner demand (command) is not the same as the will, though he does not prove it fully here.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the relationship between inner demand and will?
A) They are identical.
B) They are distinct, with will being a part of demand.
C) They are distinct, and demand is not will.
D) They are the same in essence but different in expression.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes that inner demand is not will, leaving room for further argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt a strong inner demand to do something that conflicted with your own personal desires. How does Razi's distinction between inner demand and will help you understand that experience? What does it imply about the source of that demand?
?
If inner demand is not the same as your will, then when you hear a command from God, you are not simply following your own inclination. Does this realization make divine commands feel more external and binding, or more liberating? Examine your own heart: do you sometimes equate obedience with your own will to soften its challenge?
?
Razi's logical argument shows that inner demand is a distinct reality. How does this affect your understanding of your own internal motivations? Are you more likely to attribute your inner demands to your own will rather than to an objective source? How can you cultivate awareness of the difference in your daily life?

First Proof that Inner Demand is Not Will (Razi)
First proof that command (amr) is not will (irāda) using the unbeliever example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that God's command to the unbeliever proves that inner demand (ṭalab) is distinct from will (irāda).

Core Idea
God's command exists where will does not — demand and will are distinct.

This lesson presents Razi's first proof for the distinction between God's demand (ṭalab) and His will (irāda). The argument hinges on the fact that God commands belief from those He knows will never believe, showing that command can exist without will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Pay attention to the terms 'amr', 'ṭalab', and 'irāda'.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms: إرادة (will), طلب (demand), أمر (command), معلوم (known), ممتنع (impossible), مراد (willed). Note how they relate.
These terms are central to the proof.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: God commands the unbeliever → He knows they will not believe → their belief is impossible → impossible cannot be willed → command exists without will → demand and will are different.
This chain is the 'strong point' Razi mentions.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for divine attributes. If command and will were the same, God would be inconsistent. This proof preserves God's transcendence.
Think about how this fits into the broader discussion of divine attributes.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasp the core logic.
Use only the information from this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن الإرادة لوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أنه لا نزاع في أنه تعالى أمر بإيمان من يعلم أنه لا يؤمن، أن يقال : إنه يريد الإيمان منه، لأنه تعالى عالم الغيب، فإن خلاف المعلوم ممتنع الوقوع، وكل ما كان ممتنع الوقوع لا يكون مراد الوقوع، فلما تحقق الأمر والطلب مع عدم الإرادة علمنا أن ماهية هذا الطلب مغايرة لماهية الإرادة، وهذه النكتة هي النكتة القوية في إثبات هذا المطلوب.
We say: It is not permissible that it be expression for the will (irāda) due to aspects. The first proof: that there is no dispute that He, exalted, commanded belief from one whom He knows will not believe. It cannot be said that He wills belief from him, because He, exalted, is Knower of the unseen; for what is contrary to what is known is impossible of occurrence, and everything the occurrence of which is impossible cannot be willed to occur. So when command and demand are realized along with absence of will, we know that the essence (māhiyya) of this demand is different from the essence of will. This point is the strong point in establishing this sought matter.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) that the inner demand (ṭalab) is not identical with will (irāda). Razi argues from the case of God's command to those whom He knows will never believe, such as Abū Jahl or Abū Lahab. Since God knows with certain knowledge that they will not believe, belief from them is impossible in reality (because what is known cannot be otherwise). That which is impossible to occur cannot be willed to occur. Yet God commands them to believe (the command is real). Therefore, the command exists without will. Hence the essence of the command/demand is different from the essence of will. Razi calls this the 'strong point' (al-nukta al-qawiyya) in establishing the distinction.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
طلب (ṭalab)
أمر (amr)
معلوم (ma‘lūm)
ممتنع (mumtani‘)
مراد (murād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if God commands something, He must will it.
Correction: Razi shows that command can exist without will, as in the case of the unbeliever.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to will belief from one He knows will not believe?
A) Because God never wills disbelief.
B) Because what is known to be impossible cannot be willed to occur.
C) Because will and command are the same thing.
D) Because God changes His will based on human actions.

Key Takeaway
The case of the unbeliever demonstrates that God's command (amr) is distinct from His will (irāda).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God commanded the unbeliever to believe, though He knew he would not. Are there commands of God you are ignoring because you think your obedience is impossible? How does this passage expose that excuse?
?
The distinction between command and will means God can demand something of you even when He does not will it to happen from you. What does that imply about your responsibility to obey regardless of your feelings or outcomes?
?
If God's command stands independent of His will for your action, then your failure to obey is not because God 'didn't will it' but because you refused. What command are you currently treating as optional under the guise of 'God knows I'm weak'?

Command Without Will: Razi's Second Proof
Razi gives three examples of command without will and the converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that command (amr) is distinct from will (irāda), through three examples of command without will and the converse case.

Core Idea
Command and will are not the same; you can command without willing the action.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a second proof that the inner demand (ṭalab) is not identical to will. He provides three concrete examples where a command is issued without the commander genuinely willing the commanded action, and then notes the converse—will without command—which is intuitively clear. The lesson concludes that these two realities are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once through, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Razi first states his thesis, then gives three examples, then the converse, then the conclusion.
Pay attention to how each example illustrates command without will.
2
Analyze the three examples
Identify each example—sultan's official, master and slave, Prophet and disbelievers—and ask: In each case, why does the commander not will the commanded action? What is the underlying principle?
The third example is the strongest for the divine attribute issue.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that inner demand (ṭalab) and will are different. Why is this distinction important for understanding divine attributes and prophethood?
This proof refutes those who conflate command with will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنه قد يوجد الأمر بدون الإرادة، وقد توجد الإرادة بدون الأمر: أما أنه قد يوجد الأمر بدون الإرادة ففي صور : إحداها : أن السلطان إذا أمر زيدًا أن يأمر عَمرًا بشيء فقد يكون زيد كارها تصور ذلك الفعل من عمرو إلا أنه يأمره لأجل أن السلطان أمره بذلك، فههنا الأمر حصل والإرادة غير حاصلة. ثانيها : ما ذكره أصحابنا - رحمهم الله - من أن الرجل إذا ضرب عبده فشكا العبد ذلك إلى السلطان، فقال السلطان: لم ضربت عبدك ؟ فقال : إنه لا يطيعني، ثم لأجل هذا العذر قال للعبد : افعل كذا وكذا ، فالأمر قد حصل ههنا، مع أنه لا يريد إقدامه على ذلك الفعل؛ لأنه لو أقدم عليه لما تمهد عذره عند السلطان. وثالثها: أنه تعالى لما أخبر عن أبي جهل وأبي لهب أنهما يموتان على الكفر، والنبي ما كان يريد الإيمان منهما ؛ لأن من لوازم صدور الإيمان منهما دخول الكذب في كلام الله تعالى ومريد الشيء مريد لما هو من لوازمه ومن ضروراته، فثبت أنه ما كان يريد الإيمان منهما، وكان يأمرهما بالإيمان، فعلمنا أن الأمر قد يحصل بدون الإرادة، وأما أن الإرادة قد تحصل بدون الأمر فظاهر، فإن الإنسان قد يقول : أريد منك أن تفعل هذا إلا أني لا آمرك به. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن هذا الطلب القائم بالنفس والاقتضاء الموجود في القلب أمر مغاير للإرادة. وأما الخبر الذهني فنقول : لا شك أن قولنا باللسان: قام زيد، وضرب عمرو، يدل على حكم ذهني وإسناد عقلي، وهذا الحكم الذهني والإسناد العقلي ظاهر أنه ليس من جنس القدرة والإرادة، إنما الذي يقع فيه الاشتباه أن يقال: إن هذا
The second proof: that command (amr) may exist without will (irāda), and will may exist without command. As for command without will, it occurs in cases: One: when a sultan commands Zayd to command ‘Amr with something, Zayd may be averse to the occurrence of that action from ‘Amr but he commands him because the sultan commanded him to do so. Here the command exists while will is not present. Two: what our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned: when a man beats his slave, and the slave complains to the sultan, the sultan says: 'Why did you beat your slave?' He replies: 'He does not obey me.' Then for the sake of this excuse, he says to the slave: 'Do such and such.' The command exists here while he does not will the slave's undertaking that action, because if he undertook it, his excuse before the sultan would not be established. Three: when He, exalted, informed about Abū Jahl and Abū Lahab that they would die upon disbelief, the Prophet did not will belief from them, because from the necessary consequences of the occurrence of belief from them would be the entry of falsehood into the speech of Allah, and the willer of a thing is the willer of its necessary consequences and requisites. So it is established that he did not will belief from them, yet he commanded them to believe. Thus we know that command may occur without will. As for will occurring without command, it is clear: a person may say 'I want you to do this but I do not command you to do it.' So by these aspects it is established that this demand (ṭalab) subsisting in the soul and the requisition (iqtiḍā') existing in the heart is something different from will. As for the mental informative (al-khabar al-dhihnī), we say: there is no doubt that our utterance with the tongue 'Zayd stood up' and '‘Amr struck' indicates a mental judgment (ḥukm dhihnī) and an intellectual attribution (isnād ‘aqlī). This mental judgment and intellectual attribution are clearly not of the genus of power (qudra) and will. The only point of confusion is to say that this…
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) that inner demand is not will. Razi first states that command can exist without will and vice versa. He gives three cases of command without will: (1) A sultan commands an official to command someone else, while the official himself does not will that action. (2) A master beats his slave; when the sultan asks why, the master cites disobedience. To avoid further blame, the master then commands the slave to do something, but he does not will him to actually do it, because if the slave obeyed, the master's excuse would be undermined. (3) Prophet Muhammad commanded Abū Jahl and Abū Lahab to believe, even though the Prophet knew they would not believe and did not will their belief, because willing their belief would entail willing its necessary consequence—that God's prior information about their disbelief would be false, which is impossible. Thus command exists without will. Then Razi gives the converse: will without command is obvious—one can wish another to do something without commanding him. He concludes that the inner demand (ṭalab) and requisition (iqtiḍā') are distinct from will. Finally, he briefly turns to the mental report (al-khabar al-dhihnī), stating that it is a mental judgment and attribution, clearly not power or will. The passage ends abruptly; the original text likely continues to discuss how one might mistakenly identify the mental report with something else.

Key Terms
أمر (amr)
إرادة (irāda)
سلطان (sulṭān)
مريد (murīd)
طلب (ṭalab)
اقتضاء (iqtiḍā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a command is issued, the commander must will the commanded action.
Correction: Razi shows that command can be issued without willing the action, as in the three examples.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what is the common feature of all three examples of command without will?
A) The commander is forced to command against his will.
B) The commander does not want the commanded action to actually occur.
C) The commander is ignorant of the consequences.
D) The commander changes his mind after issuing the command.

Key Takeaway
Command (amr) and will (irāda) are distinct: one can command without willing the commanded action, and vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you command someone to do something—like your child, employee, or even yourself—do you genuinely will the action, or are you sometimes issuing commands out of obligation or appearance? How might you bring your will into alignment with your commands?
?
The Prophet commanded Abu Jahl to believe even though he knew it would not happen. Are you willing to command good even when you see no hope of acceptance? What holds you back?
?
Have you ever said 'I want to do this' but never commanded yourself to act? What does that reveal about the gap between your desires and your actual demands on yourself?
